Objection and Answer on Form's Need for Matter
Objection and answer on form's need for matter and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity regarding form's need for matter is answered by distinguishing different modes and degrees of constitution.

Core Idea
Form's need for matter does not cause circularity if constitution occurs at different degrees.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection and answer concerning the relationship between form and matter in composition. The objector claims that if form needs matter, then form cannot be part of the agent because of circularity. The author refutes this by carefully analyzing the modes of constitution and showing that circularity is avoided when forms operate at different degrees.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Identify the objector's claim that form's need for matter leads to circularity.
Notice the structure: 'If it is said...' introduces the objection.
2
Understand the answer's first part: denial of circularity
The author responds by saying 'there is no circularity'. Why? Because the individuation of form and matter can be explained without reciprocal need.
The key is that form's need for matter is not at the same level.
3
Examine the two modes of constitution
The author discusses two ways matter could be constituted by two forms: independently (each form as a separate constituent) or by combination (the aggregate as constituent). The first is impossible, the second is possible.
This is about the relationship between forms and matter.
4
Learn about the two degrees of dependence
Matter may depend on forms in two degrees: directly on a corporeal form, and indirectly on a specific form through the corporeal form. This avoids circularity because the dependence is not mutual at the same level.
Example: matter → corporeal form → specific form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لما احتاجت الصورة إلى المادة امتنع كونها جزءاً من فاعلها للزوم الدور لما زعموا أن تشخص الصورة يكون بالمحل المعين، ومن حيث هو قابل لتشخصها وتشخص المحل يكون بالصورة المطلقة، ومن حيث هي فاعل لتشخصه فلا دور ولا تتقوم المادة بصورتين في درجة، إما بطريق الاستقلال بأن يكون كل منهما مقوماً فظاهر لأن تقومها لكل منهما يستلزم عن الأخرى، وإما بطريق الاجتماع فلأن المقوم حينئذ يكون هو المجموع إلى كل واحد والمجموع أمر واحد ويجوز تقوم المادة بصورتين في درجتين كالصورة الجسمية والنوعية للمادة بمعنى أنها تفتقر في وجودها إلى الصورة الجسمية المفتقرة إلى الصورة النوعية فيقع افتقار المادة إليها في الدرجة الثانية.
If it is said: Since form needs matter, it is impossible for it to be a part of its agent because of circularity, because they claim that the individuation of form is by the specific locus, and insofar as it is receptive to its individuation, and the individuation of the locus is by the absolute form, and insofar as it is an agent for its individuation, there is no circularity. And matter is not constituted by two forms in one degree; either independently—where each is a constituent, then it is obvious because its being constituted by each entails independence from the other—or by combination, because then the constituent is the aggregate, and the aggregate is one thing, and it is possible for matter to be constituted by two forms in two degrees, like the corporeal and specific forms for matter, meaning that it depends in its existence on the corporeal form, which depends on the specific form, so matter's dependence on them occurs in the second degree.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the composition of form and matter. The objector argues that if form requires matter, then form cannot be a part of its own agent (the composite) because that would lead to circularity: form is individuated by matter, and matter is individuated by form. The answer denies circularity by explaining that matter is not constituted by two forms in the same degree. The author then distinguishes two modes: independent constitution (where each form is a constituent independently, which is impossible) and combined constitution (where the aggregate is the constituent, which is possible). He further allows two degrees: matter depends on a corporeal form, which in turn depends on a specific form, so matter's dependence on the specific form is indirect (second degree). This avoids circularity.

Key Terms
صورة
مادة
دور
تشخص
مقوم
درجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any need of form for matter implies circularity.
Correction: Circularity is avoided when the dependence is at different degrees (e.g., matter depends on form indirectly through an intermediary).

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, in how many ways can matter be constituted by two forms?
A) One way: independently.
B) Two ways: independently and by combination.
C) Three ways: independently, by combination, and by degrees.
D) Only by combination.

Key Takeaway
The need of form for matter does not entail circularity, because form can be a constituent at a different degree from matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your life where you rely on circular justifications—'I do this because I am this way, and I am this way because I do this'—rather than accepting a deeper, non-circular foundation? What would it cost you to break that cycle by acknowledging a higher source?
?
When you examine your relationship with God, do you ever fall into the trap of expecting reciprocation in the same degree, as if your dependence and His mercy are on the same level? How does realizing the 'different degrees' of dependence free you from expecting a transactional relationship with your Creator?
?
Consider a belief you hold about yourself that seems to be caught in a loop: 'I am not good enough because I fail, and I fail because I am not good enough.' Could this be a misplaced certainty about a single degree of constitution? What would change if you allowed yourself to see your flaws as part of a larger, ordered structure of growth rather than a static circular identity?

Form and Matter in Artificial Composites
Defines form and matter in artificial composites with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of form and matter as used in artificial composites, where form is an accident and matter is a substance.

Core Idea
In artifacts, form is a configuration that is an accident; matter is its underlying substance.

Classical theologians distinguished between natural and artificial composites. In artificial objects like a bed or sword, the form is not a substance but an accident added to pre-existing matter. This passage lays out that foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the technical terms like 'hay'a' (configuration) and 'arad' (accident).
2
Identify the definitions of form and matter
The passage defines form as the configuration in a substrate, and matter as the locus of that configuration. Compare with the example of whiteness in body.
Form and matter here are used homonymously with the natural sense.
3
Examine the example of the bed
The bed-form produced by the carpenter is an accident subsisting in the planks. This clarifies that in artifacts, form is not a substance.
4
Understand why this avoids the sword objection
The passage briefly mentions that this definition sidesteps the objection that the sword-form does not necessitate the sword. This will be elaborated in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستغناء. قال: وقد يقال لكل هيئة في قابل وجداني بالذات أو بالاعتبار والمادة لمحلها كالبياض والجسم ويشبه أن يكون مثل السيف والسرير من هذا القبيل، إذ الصانع لم يحدث فيه جواهر بل هيئة وحينئذ لا يرد الاعتراض بأن الهيئة السيفية ليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل كما في الحجر. وأما جواب الإمام بأنا لا نعني أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب بل إن الصورة الشخصية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادة الشخصية فيشعر بأن الصورة ههنا بالمعنى السابق على أن السيف مثلا اسم للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الجوهر والعارض الذي هو الهيئة. كل من الصورة والمادة يقال بالاشتراك بمعنى غير ما سبق، فالصورة الهيئة الحاصلة في أمر قابل له وحدة بحسب الذات أو بحسب الاعتبار، والمادة لمحل تلك الهيئة كالبياض والجسم وبهذا الاعتبار يصح إضافة كل منهما إلى الآخر. والظاهر أن إطلاق الصورة والمادة في المركبات الصناعية مثل السيف والسرير والبيت يكون بهذا المعنى لأن الهيئة التي أحدثها النجار وسموها الصورة السريرية إنما هي عرض قائم بالخشبات لا جوهر حال فيها، وكذا صورة السيف والبيت وعلى هذا يندفع اعتراض الإمام على تفسير العلة الصورية، بأن الهيئة السيفية صورة للسيف وليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل، إذ قد يكون في خشب أو حجر ولا سيف. وأجاب الإمام بأنا لا نعني بوجوب المركب مع الصورة أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب، بل إن الصورة الخشبية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادته الشخصية فإنها لا توجبه، بل قد تكون بعينها مادة شيء آخر، والصورة الحاصلة في الحجر ليست بعينها الصورة الحالة في الحديد بل بنوعها وهذا يشعر بأن المراد بالصورة في المركبات الصناعية أيضاً الجزء الذي يجب المركب معه بالفعل، ولا يستقيم إلا إذا جعلنا السيف مثلاً اسماً للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الحديد، والعارض الذي هو الهيئة، فيكون كل منهما داخلاً فيه ووجوبه مع الأول بالقوة، ومع الثاني بالفعل.
Independence. He said: And it may be said that every configuration (hay'a) in a substrate is found either essentially or by consideration, and matter is its locus, like whiteness and body. And it seems that things like the sword and bed are of this type, since the artisan did not create substances in them but only a configuration. Then the objection does not arise that the sword-configuration is not such that the sword actually necessarily accompanies it, as in stone. As for the answer of the Imam (al-Razi), that we do not mean that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the particular sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its particular matter—this indicates that form here is in the previous sense, that the sword, for example, is a name for the composite of the substrate (jawhar) and the accident ('arad) which is the configuration. Both 'form' and 'matter' are used homonymously in a different sense from before. Form is the configuration occurring in something receptive that has unity essentially or by consideration, and matter is the locus of that configuration, like whiteness and body. In this respect, it is correct to attribute each to the other. It appears that the application of form and matter in artificial composites like the sword, bed, and house is in this sense, because the configuration that the carpenter produces and calls the bed-form is an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance indwelling them. Similarly for the form of the sword and house. On this basis, the objection of the Imam against the interpretation of the formal cause is repelled—that the sword-configuration is a form for the sword but not such that the sword actually necessarily accompanies it, since it may exist in wood or stone without a sword. The Imam answered: We do not mean by the necessity of the composite with the form that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the particular wooden? sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its particular matter, which does not necessitate it; indeed, the same matter may be matter for something else. And the form occurring in stone is not identical to the form existing in iron but only in type. This indicates that the meaning of form in artificial composites is also the part with which the composite actually necessarily comes. And this is only consistent if we make the sword, for example, a name for the composite of the substrate which is iron and the accident which is the configuration; then each is included in it, and its necessity with the first is potential, and with the second is actual.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of form and matter in artificial composites. It begins by stating that every configuration in a substrate is either essentially or by consideration considered form, and matter is its locus, like whiteness in body. Artifacts like swords and beds have only a configuration (form) added to pre-existing substances; the artisan does not create new substances. This avoids the objection that the sword-form does not actually necessitate the sword (as it can be in wood or stone). The author then presents the Imam's (al-Razi's) answer: the particular form (not the type) necessitates the particular composite. The passage then redefines form and matter homonymously: form is the configuration in a receptive unit, matter is its locus. In artificial composites, the form is an accident (like the bed-form in wood) and the matter is the substrate (e.g., iron for a sword). The Imam's objection about the formal cause is refuted by clarifying that the necessity is for the particular composite with the particular form. Finally, the author concludes that the sword is a composite of matter (iron) and form (configuration), and its existence is potential with the matter alone and actual with the form.

Key Terms
هيئة
عارض
جوهر
صورة سريرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone may think that form in artifacts is a substance, like the soul in a body.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the carpenter's form (bed-form) is an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the form of a bed?
A) A substance indwelling the wood.
B) An accident subsisting in the wood.
C) The wood itself.
D) A separate entity.

Key Takeaway
In artificial composites, form is an accident and matter is its substance; the artisan only imposes a configuration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You impose configurations on the 'matter' of your time, energy, and relationships every day. Which of these configurations are you mistaking for something substantial and permanent? Will they survive when the matter is gone?
?
The artisan only creates an accident, not a new substance. What in your life are you laboring to produce that is merely a configuration—a shape, a reputation, a routine—while neglecting the substance of your soul? What would it take to shift your focus?
?
Al-Razi teaches that only the particular form necessitates the particular composite. You are not a generic 'type' of Muslim—you are a unique composite of actions and states. Are you living as a particular, irreplaceable servant before Allah, or are you content with a generic shape?

Refuting the Sword Objection: Particular Form vs. Type
Refutes the objection about the sword-form by distinguishing type from particular form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi answers the objection that the sword-form does not necessitate the sword, by distinguishing type from particular form.

Core Idea
The particular form, not the type, necessitates the particular composite.

A common objection against the formal cause in artifacts is that the sword-form does not always produce a sword—it can exist in wood or stone. In this passage, the author presents al-Razi's nuanced answer: it is the particular sword-form that necessitates the particular sword, not the type of form.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that the sword-form (hay'a sayfiyya) is not such that the sword actually follows from it, citing examples of wood or stone.
2
Understand al-Razi's answer
Al-Razi clarifies that the necessity is for the particular form (e.g., the specific shape in iron) with the particular composite (that sword), not the type of form.
This resolves the objection because the form in the stone is only similar in type, not identical.
3
Examine the resolution
The passage explains that the sword is a composite of matter (iron) and form (configuration). Its existence is potential with matter alone and actual with the form.
4
Connect to the broader concept
This clarifies the formal cause: the form is the part that actually necessitates the composite's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستغناء. قال: وقد يقال لكل هيئة في قابل وجداني بالذات أو بالاعتبار والمادة لمحلها كالبياض والجسم ويشبه أن يكون مثل السيف والسرير من هذا القبيل، إذ الصانع لم يحدث فيه جواهر بل هيئة وحينئذ لا يرد الاعتراض بأن الهيئة السيفية ليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل كما في الحجر. وأما جواب الإمام بأنا لا نعني أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب بل إن الصورة الشخصية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادة الشخصية فيشعر بأن الصورة ههنا بالمعنى السابق على أن السيف مثلا اسم للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الجوهر والعارض الذي هو الهيئة. كل من الصورة والمادة يقال بالاشتراك بمعنى غير ما سبق، فالصورة الهيئة الحاصلة في أمر قابل له وحدة بحسب الذات أو بحسب الاعتبار، والمادة لمحل تلك الهيئة كالبياض والجسم وبهذا الاعتبار يصح إضافة كل منهما إلى الآخر. والظاهر أن إطلاق الصورة والمادة في المركبات الصناعية مثل السيف والسرير والبيت يكون بهذا المعنى لأن الهيئة التي أحدثها النجار وسموها الصورة السريرية إنما هي عرض قائم بالخشبات لا جوهر حال فيها، وكذا صورة السيف والبيت وعلى هذا يندفع اعتراض الإمام على تفسير العلة الصورية، بأن الهيئة السيفية صورة للسيف وليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل، إذ قد يكون في خشب أو حجر ولا سيف. وأجاب الإمام بأنا لا نعني بوجوب المركب مع الصورة أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب، بل إن الصورة الخشبية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادته الشخصية فإنها لا توجبه، بل قد تكون بعينها مادة شيء آخر، والصورة الحاصلة في الحجر ليست بعينها الصورة الحالة في الحديد بل بنوعها وهذا يشعر بأن المراد بالصورة في المركبات الصناعية أيضاً الجزء الذي يجب المركب معه بالفعل، ولا يستقيم إلا إذا جعلنا السيف مثلاً اسماً للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الحديد، والعارض الذي هو الهيئة، فيكون كل منهما داخلاً فيه ووجوبه مع الأول بالقوة، ومع الثاني بالفعل.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any instance of a sword-form (e.g., in wood) should produce a sword.
Correction: Only the particular form in the appropriate matter (iron) necessitates the sword; the form in wood is only similar in type.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's answer, what necessitates the actual sword?
A) The type of sword-form.
B) The particular sword-form in the matter.
C) The matter alone.
D) The configuration in stone.

Key Takeaway
The formal cause in artifacts is the particular form that actualizes the composite; the same type in different matter does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, have you ever assumed that because something looks like a good deed (the type) it is automatically accepted, neglecting your particular intention and circumstances? How does this lesson warn you against that mistake?
?
The same matter (e.g., your time, talents) can be shaped by different forms—some actualizing good and others not. What 'form' are you currently impressing on the matter of your daily actions? Is it actually producing the composite you desire or merely a resemblance?
?
If the particular form necessitates the composite, then your unique situation and choices are decisive. Are you relying on general principles without applying them to your specific 'matter'? What would change if you took this lesson seriously in one concrete area of your life today?

The Definitions of Form and Matter
Al-Taftazani defines form and matter with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental definitions of form (ṣūra) and matter (mādda) as used in Islamic philosophy and kalām.

Core Idea
Form is an accident; matter is its locus.

Al-Taftazani begins by establishing the conceptual foundation for understanding form and matter. These terms are not only central to physics and metaphysics but also to the analysis of artificial objects. The passage defines them clearly before applying them to examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Notice the terms 'hay'a' and 'mādda'.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines form (hay'a) as an accident occurring in a subject capable of unity. Matter is the locus of that form.
Example: whiteness (form) and body (matter).
3
Reflect on the 'by consideration' phrase
The phrase 'by essence or by consideration' indicates that unity can be either literal or conceptual. Consider how this applies to artificial composites.
This nuance is important for later lessons.
4
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint to ensure you can distinguish form from matter based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهيئة الحاصلة في أمر قابل له وحدة بحسب الذات أو بحسب الاعتبار، والمادة لمحل تلك الهيئة كالبياض والجسم وبهذا الاعتبار يصح إضافة كل منهما إلى الآخر.
والظاهر أن إطلاق الصورة والمادة في المركبات الصناعية مثل السيف والسرير والبيت يكون بهذا المعنى لأن الهيئة التي أحدثها النجار وسموها الصورة السريرية إنما هي عرض قائم بالخشبات لا جوهر حال فيها، وكذا صورة السيف والبيت. وعلى هذا يندفع اعتراض الإمام على تفسير العلة الصورية، بأن الهيئة السيفية صورة للسيف وليست مما يجب معها السيف بالفعل، إذ قد يكون في خشب أو حجر ولا سيف. وأجاب الإمام بأنا لا نعني بوجوب المركب مع الصورة أن نوع الصورة يوجب المركب، بل إن الصورة الخشبية السيفية مثلا توجب ذلك السيف بخلاف مادته الشخصية فإنها لا توجبه، بل قد تكون بعينها مادة شيء آخر، والصورة الحاصلة في الحجر ليست بعينها الصورة الحالة في الحديد بل بنوعها. وهذا يشعر بأن المراد بالصورة في المركبات الصناعية أيضاً الجزء الذي يجب المركب معه بالفعل، ولا يستقيم إلا إذا جعلنا السيف مثلاً اسماً للمركب من المعروض الذي هو الحديد، والعارض الذي هو الهيئة، فيكون كل منهما داخلاً فيه ووجوبه مع الأول بالقوة، ومع الثاني بالفعل.
The form is the accident occurring in a subject capable of unity either by essence or by consideration, and the matter is the locus of that form, like whiteness and body; in this respect it is valid to attribute each to the other. And it is apparent that the application of form and matter to artificial composites such as the sword, the bed, and the house is in this sense, because the shape which the carpenter produces and calls the bed-form is but an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance residing in them; likewise the form of the sword and the house. And on this basis the objection of al-Imam against the interpretation of the formal cause falls away, namely that the sword-shape is a form of the sword yet the sword does not necessarily exist with it actually, for it may be in wood or stone and there is no sword. And al-Imam answered that we do not mean by the necessity of the composite with the form that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that the wooden sword-form, for example, necessitates that particular sword, unlike its individual matter which does not necessitate it; indeed, it might be the very same matter of something else. And the form occurring in stone is not literally the form present in iron but is of its type. And this indicates that what is meant by form in artificial composites is also the part with which the composite necessarily exists actually, and it is only sound if we make the sword, for example, a name for the composite of the subject which is the iron and the accident which is the shape, so that each of them is included in it, and its existence with the first is potential, and with the second actual.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'form' (ṣūra) and 'matter' (mādda) as used in philosophical and kalām contexts, and then applies them to artificial composites. The author begins by stating that form is the accident (ʿaraḍ) that occurs in a subject capable of unity, either essentially (bi-ḥasabi al-dhāt) or by consideration (bi-ḥasabi al-iʿtibār). Matter is the locus (maḥall) of that form, like whiteness in a body. Thus, form and matter can be attributed to each other in this sense. Then the author argues that in artificial composites like a sword, bed, or house, the terms are used similarly: the shape (hayʾa) produced by the carpenter is an accident subsisting in the wood, not a substance. This resolves the objection of al-Imam (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) against the interpretation of the formal cause (al-ʿilla al-ṣūriyya). The objection was: the sword-shape is a form of the sword, yet the sword does not necessarily exist when that shape is present, since the shape could be in wood or stone without constituting a sword. The author reports Imam's answer: they do not mean that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that a specific form—e.g., the wooden sword-form—necessitates that particular sword, whereas the individual matter does not necessitate it; the same matter could be used for something else. Moreover, the form in stone is not identical to the form in iron but of its type. This indicates that 'form' in artificial composites means the part with which the composite actually exists, and this works only if we consider the sword as a composite of the subject (iron) and the accident (shape), so that the composite exists potentially with the matter alone and actually with the form.

Key Terms

مادة
بياض
جسم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is matter?
A) The accident that inheres in a subject
B) The locus or subject of the form
C) The essence of a thing
D) The shape of an object

Key Takeaway
Form is an accident; matter is the subject that receives it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a quality you dislike about yourself—a habit or a flaw. According to this lesson, is it an accident (form) or part of your essential substance (matter)? How does this distinction change how you would approach changing it? Be honest: what action can you take today to address the accidental nature of that flaw?
?
Identify a relationship in your life that may be based on 'unity by consideration' rather than essential connection. How does realizing this affect your responsibility and commitment in that relationship? Will you continue to invest in it as if it were essential?

Form and Matter in Artificial Composites
Al-Taftazani applies form and matter to artificial composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms form and matter apply to man-made objects like a sword, bed, and house.

Core Idea
In artifacts, the form is the shape produced by the craftsman, which is an accident.

Al-Taftazani extends the definitions to artificial composites. He argues that the shape a carpenter produces in a bed is an accident, not a substance, and similarly for other artifacts. This clarifies that form in these contexts is not a substantial form but an accidental one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the carpenter
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the examples: sword, bed, house.
Notice the use of 'hay'a' for shape.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that the shape produced by the carpenter is an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in the planks, not a substance (jawhar).
This aligns with the previous definition of form as an accident.
3
Connect to the initial definition
Consider how this application matches the earlier definition: form is an accident, matter is its locus.
The bed's matter is the wood, its form is the bed-shape.
4
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint to confirm you recognize the form in artifacts as an accident.

Source Text

And it is apparent that the application of form and matter to artificial composites such as the sword, the bed, and the house is in this sense, because the shape which the carpenter produces and calls the bed-form is but an accident subsisting in the planks, not a substance residing in them; likewise the form of the sword and the house. Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'form' (ṣūra) and 'matter' (mādda) as used in philosophical and kalām contexts, and then applies them to artificial composites. The author begins by stating that form is the accident (ʿaraḍ) that occurs in a subject capable of unity, either essentially (bi-ḥasabi al-dhāt) or by consideration (bi-ḥasabi al-iʿtibār). Matter is the locus (maḥall) of that form, like whiteness in a body. Thus, form and matter can be attributed to each other in this sense. Then the author argues that in artificial composites like a sword, bed, or house, the terms are used similarly: the shape (hayʾa) produced by the carpenter is an accident subsisting in the wood, not a substance. This resolves the objection of al-Imam (likely Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) against the interpretation of the formal cause (al-ʿilla al-ṣūriyya). The objection was: the sword-shape is a form of the sword, yet the sword does not necessarily exist when that shape is present, since the shape could be in wood or stone without constituting a sword. The author reports Imam's answer: they do not mean that the type of form necessitates the composite, but rather that a specific form—e.g., the wooden sword-form—necessitates that particular sword, whereas the individual matter does not necessitate it; the same matter could be used for something else. Moreover, the form in stone is not identical to the form in iron but of its type. This indicates that 'form' in artificial composites means the part with which the composite actually exists, and this works only if we consider the sword as a composite of the subject (iron) and the accident (shape), so that the composite exists potentially with the matter alone and actually with the form.

?

?

?

Refuting the Objection on Formal Cause
Al-Taftazani reports and refutes an objection regarding formal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Imam's objection regarding the necessity of the composite with the form and al-Taftazani's solution.

Core Idea
The specific form necessitates the specific composite; matter does not.

Al-Taftazani addresses an objection from al-Imam (likely al-Razi). The objection is that if form is what makes the composite actual, then why doesn't a sword shape in wood produce a sword? The answer distinguishes between specific form and type of form, and explains that the composite exists potentially with matter and actually with form.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage up to '... ولا سيف'. Understand the problem: the sword-shape alone does not guarantee a sword.
The objection is based on the example of a shape in wood or stone.
2
Analyze al-Imam's answer
Read the answer: they do not mean the type of form necessitates, but the specific form (e.g., wooden sword-form) necessitates that particular composite.
Notice the distinction between type and individual.
3
Understand the matter's role
The matter does not necessitate the composite because it can be used for other things. The form in stone is not identical to that in iron.
This reinforces that form is the actualizing principle.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that form is the part that makes the composite actual, and the composite exists potentially with matter and actually with form.
This requires defining the artifact as a composite of matter (subject) and form (accident).
5
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint to confirm you understand the distinction between type and specific form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that the specific form (e.g., wooden sword-form) necessitates a specific composite. What 'forms' are you repeatedly subjecting your heart and mind to—entertainment, thoughts, habits—and what composite are they producing in you? Are you content with that result?
?

Accidents Specific to Living Beings
The author identifies accidents specific to living beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what accidents are specific to living beings according to the theologians, and why life and its concomitants form a distinct category.

Core Idea
Life, knowledge, power, will, speech, and perception are accidents unique to living beings.

This passage opens the classification of accidents. The author distinguishes between accidents that belong only to living beings and those that can occur in non-living things. By focusing on the first category, we see how the theologians define life and its inseparable attributes. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping later debates about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text once to hear the author's classification. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the specific accidents
Notice the list: life, knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions. Why are these grouped together?
3
Understand 'sensation'
The author explains perceptions as sensation by the senses. Reflect on how this connects to the living being's capacity for awareness.
4
Contrast with non-specific accidents
Though not covered in this lesson, keep in mind that the following will discuss accidents that are not specific, like motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرض إما أن يكون مختصاً بالحي كالحياة، وما يتبعها من العلم والقدرة، والإرادة، والكلام، والإدراكات أعني الإحساس بالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، وإما ألا يكون مختصة وهي الأكوان والمحسوسات. فالأكوان أربعة: الاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون، وزاد بعضهم الكون الأول، وهو الحصول في الحيز عقيب العدم والمحسوسات المدركات بالصبر[؟]، أو
And the accident is either specific to the living being, such as life and what follows it of knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions – I mean sensation by the external and internal senses – or it is not specific, and they are the modes (akwān) and the sensible qualities. So the modes are four: conjunction, separation, motion, and rest. Some added the first mode, which is obtaining in a location after non-existence, and the sensible qualities perceived by sight[؟], or
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage classifies accidents (aʿrāḍ). It divides them into two categories: (1) accidents specific to the living being (al-ḥayy), such as life (ḥayāt) and its concomitants: knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions (sensation via external and internal senses); (2) accidents that are not specific, which are the akwān (modes) and the maḥsūsāt (sensible qualities). The akwān are four primary modes: conjunction (ijtimāʿ), separation (iftirāq), motion (ḥaraka), and rest (sukūn). Some theologians add a fifth: the first mode (al-kawn al-awwal), which is obtaining in a location after non-existence. The passage ends mid-sentence, listing the sensible qualities as perceived by the senses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All accidents can occur in both living and non-living things.
Correction: The passage states that life, knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions are specific to living beings.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as an accident specific to living beings?
A) Life
B) Knowledge
C) Motion
D) Will

Key Takeaway
Life and its concomitants—knowledge, power, will, speech, and perception—are considered accidents unique to living beings in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your life is an accident that can cease at any moment, how are you living as if it is permanent? What would change if you truly internalized that your knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions are temporary gifts from Allah, not intrinsic to your self?
?
The passage lists speech as an accident of living beings. When you speak, do you recognize that your speech is a borrowed attribute from Allah? How does that affect your responsibility for every word?
?
Your internal senses (imagination, memory) are categorized as accidents. How often do you reflect that these too are created and can be taken away? What would you prioritize if you knew your memory could vanish?

The Primary Modes (Akwan) and Sensible Qualities
The author enumerates the primary modes (akwan) and introduces sensible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four (or five) akwan and their definitions, and how they relate to the sensible qualities.

Core Idea
Motion, rest, conjunction, separation, and sometimes the first mode form the five primary accidents of bodies.

After distinguishing accidents specific to living beings, the author turns to the other category: accidents that are not specific. These are the akwan (modes) and the sensible qualities. The akwan are fundamental to understanding how bodies change and interact. This passage lists the four primary akwan and notes a fifth variant. It also begins to mention sensible qualities, but the text cuts off.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on akwan
Read the Arabic text for this lesson. Focus on the enumeration of the akwan.
2
Identify the five akwan
Note the four standard ones: conjunction, separation, motion, rest. Then see the additional first mode: obtaining in a location after non-existence.
3
Understand the first mode
The first mode (kawn awwal) is a special case of rest? It describes the initial location of a body after it comes into existence.
4
Consider sensible qualities
The passage mentions al-mahsūsāt (sensible qualities) but does not list them. Think about what they might be (colors, sounds, etc.).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرض إما أن يكون مختصاً بالحي كالحياة، وما يتبعها من العلم والقدرة، والإرادة، والكلام، والإدراكات أعني الإحساس بالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، وإما ألا يكون مختصة وهي الأكوان والمحسوسات. فالأكوان أربعة: الاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون، وزاد بعضهم الكون الأول، وهو الحصول في الحيز عقيب العدم والمحسوسات المدركات بالصبر[؟]، أو

Commentary
This passage classifies accidents (aʿrāḍ). It divides them into two categories: (1) accidents specific to the living being (al-ḥayy), such as life (ḥayāt) and its concomitants: knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions (sensation via external and internal senses); (2) accidents that are not specific, which are the akwān (modes) and the maḥsūsāt (sensible qualities). The akwān are four primary modes: conjunction (ijtimāʿ), separation (iftirāq), motion (ḥaraka), and rest (sukūn). Some theologians add a fifth: the first mode (al-kawn al-awwal), which is obtaining in a location after non-existence. The passage ends mid-sentence, listing the sensible qualities as perceived by the senses.

Accidents Specific to Living Beings
Sanusi lists accidents specific to living beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which accidents are specific to living beings (al-ḥayy) and how they derive from life.

Core Idea
Life and its attributes are exclusive to the living.

In this passage, Sanusi begins classifying accidents (aʿrāḍ) by dividing them into two broad categories. The first category comprises accidents that are unique to living beings—life itself and the attributes that necessarily accompany it. This lesson explores that first category and its components.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification opening
Read the first part of the passage that introduces the division of accidents into specific and non-specific. Focus on the phrase 'either specific to the living or not'.
2
Identify the specific accidents
List the accidents that are specific to the living: life, knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions (sensation). Notice that perceptions are further defined as sensations through external and internal senses.
3
Understand the relationship
Reflect on why these accidents are called 'specific to the living'. They inhere in living beings and are inseparable from life in the sense that only a living being can possess them.
4
Prepare for the next category
Note that the passage then transitions to non-specific accidents, which will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعرض إما أن يكون مختصاً بالحي كالحياة ، وما يتبعها من العلم والقدرة، والإرادة، والكلام، والإدراكات أعني الإحساس بالحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، وإما ألا يكون مختصاً وهي الأكوان والمحسوسات. فالأكوان أربعة : الاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون، وزاد بعضهم الكون الأول، وهو الحصول في الحيز عقيب العدم والمحسوسات المدركات بالصبر، أو السمع، أو الشم، أو الذوق ، أو اللمس، على ما سيجيء تفصيلها، وجعل بعضهم لذاته بمعنى أنه لا الأكوان من المبصرات [؟]
And the accident (ʿaraḍ) is either specific to the living (al-ḥayy), like life (al-ḥayāh) and what follows it of knowledge, power, will, speech, and perceptions – meaning sensation (al-iḥsās) through the external and internal senses – or it is not specific, and these are the states (akwān) and the sensible objects (al-maḥsūsāt). The akwān are four: conjunction (al-ijtimāʿ), separation (al-iftirāq), motion (al-ḥaraka), and rest (al-sukūn). Some added the first kawn (al-kawn al-awwal), which is obtaining in a place after non-existence. And the sensible objects are perceived by sight, or hearing, or smell, or taste, or touch, as their detailed account will come. And some [?] make the akwān among the visible objects.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage classifies accidents (aʿrāḍ) according to the mutakallimun. The author presents a two-fold division: accidents that are specific to living beings (al-ḥayy) and accidents that are not specific. The first category includes life (al-ḥayāh) and the attributes that follow from it: knowledge (ʿilm), power (qudra), will (irāda), speech (kalām), and perceptions (idrākāt), which are sensations through the external and internal senses. The second category includes the akwān (states) and the maḥsūsāt (sensible objects). The akwān are four: conjunction (ijtimāʿ), separation (iftirāq), motion (ḥaraka), and rest (sukūn). Some scholars add a fifth kawn, called the first kawn (al-kawn al-awwal), which is defined as obtaining in a place after non-existence. The maḥsūsāt are objects perceived by the five senses: sight, hearing, smell, taste, and touch. The author notes that the details will come later. The final phrase, which is textually problematic, seems to indicate that some scholars consider the akwān to be among visible things (mubṣirāt).

?

Non-Specific Accidents: States and Sensible Objects
Sanusi enumerates non-specific accidents: akwan and mahsusat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of non-specific accidents: the akwān (states) and the maḥsūsāt (sensible objects), and their subdivisions.

Core Idea
Akwan and mahsusat are accidents common to all bodies, not just the living.

Having covered accidents specific to living beings, Sanusi now turns to those that are not specific. These are the akwān (states) and the maḥsūsāt (sensible objects). The akwān describe the spatial relations of bodies, while the maḥsūsāt are those perceived by the five senses. This lesson unpacks these categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the non-specific accidents section
Read the second part of the source passage, starting from 'or it is not specific' up to the end. Pay attention to the enumeration of akwān and maḥsūsāt.
2
Examine the akwān
Identify the four (or five) akwān: conjunction, separation, motion, rest, and optionally the first kawn. Understand their definitions.
3
Examine the maḥsūsāt
Note that maḥsūsāt are objects perceived by the five senses: sight, hearing, smell, taste, and touch. The passage says their detail will come later.
4
Note the textual variant
The final sentence is problematic; some scholars considered akwān as visible objects. This is a point of debate.

Definition of Accident and Its Existence in a Subject
Sanusi defines accident and explains its mode of existence in a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of an accident as something that exists in a subject, and how its existence in itself is identical to its existence in its locus.

Core Idea
An accident's existence is not separate from its subject; they are one in existence.

This passage from Sanusi establishes the foundational distinction between an accident and a substance. Before we can discuss how accidents inhere, we need a clear definition of what it means for something to exist 'in' a subject. Sanusi clarifies that the accident's existence is not like a body being in a place, but rather a unique mode of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage definition
Read the Arabic carefully and the translation. Notice how Sanusi first clarifies the meaning of 'existent not in a subject' and excludes the Necessary.
2
Identify the key contrast
The author contrasts the accident's existence in its subject with a body's existence in a place. What is the difference?
The accident's existence in itself is the same as in its subject; a body's existence in a place is additional.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why is this distinction important for understanding accidents? Consider how it affects our ability to point to the accident and the subject separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وكذلك إذا لم يقيد مثل موجود لا في موضوع، فإن معناه ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا في موضوع وليس للواجب ماهية ووجود زائد عليها. ومعنى وجود العرض في المحل أن وجوده في نفسه هو وجوده في محله بحيث تكون الإشارة إلى أحدهما إشارة إلى الآخر، بخلاف وجود الجسم في المكان فإنه أمر مغاير لوجوده في نفسه مرتب عليه زائل عنه ، عند الانتقال إلى مكان آخر، فتحقيق ذلك أن ملاقاة موجود لموجود بالتمام لا على سبيل المماسة والمجاورة، بل بحيث لا يكون بينهما تباين في الوضع، ويحصل للثاني صفة الأول، كملاقاة السواد للجسم يسمى حلولاً ، والموجود الأول حالا، والثاني محلاً. والحال قد يكون بحيث لا يقوم ولا يتحصل المحل بدونه فيسمى صورة
They said: Likewise, if it is not restricted, like 'existent not in a subject', then its meaning is a quiddity (māhiyya) which, when it exists, is not in a subject, and the Necessary does not have a quiddity and existence superadded to it. And the meaning of the existence of the accident in the locus is that its existence in itself is its existence in its locus, such that pointing to one of them is pointing to the other, unlike the existence of a body in a place, for it is something other than its existence in itself, consequent upon it, and vanishing from it upon moving to another place. The verification of this is that the meeting (mulāqāh) of one existent with another existent completely, not by way of contact (mumāssa) or adjacency (mujāwara), but such that there is no separation (tabāyun) between them in position (waḍʿ), and the second acquires the attribute of the first – like the meeting of blackness with a body – is called indwelling (ḥulūl). The first existent is the state (ḥāl), and the second is the locus (maḥall). And the state may be such that the locus does not subsist and is not constituted without it, and then it is called a form (ṣūra).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of an accident's existence in its subject and the nature of ḥulūl (indwelling). The author begins by clarifying the definition of 'existent not in a subject' (mawjūd lā fī mawḍūʿ): it means a quiddity (māhiyya) that, when it exists, is not in a subject. This excludes the Necessary (wājib), which has no quiddity separate from its existence. The key point is the existence of an accident in its locus (maḥall): the accident's existence in itself is identical to its existence in the locus – pointing to one is pointing to the other. This contrasts with a body in a place: a body's existence in a place is additional to its own existence and can cease when it moves. The author then explains ḥulūl as the complete meeting of one existent with another without physical contact or adjacency, resulting in the second acquiring the attribute of the first. The first is called the state (ḥāl), and the second is the locus (maḥall). Finally, if the locus cannot subsist without the state, that state is called a form (ṣūra).

Key Terms
عرض
محل
موجود لا في موضوع
ماهية
إشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that an accident's existence in its subject is like a body being in a place.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the accident's existence in itself is the same as its existence in its subject, whereas a body's existence in a place is additional and separable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the existence of an accident in its subject differ from the existence of a body in a place?
A) The accident's existence is separate from its subject
B) The accident's existence in itself is the same as its existence in its subject
C) The body's existence in a place is identical to its own existence
D) The accident's existence is not subject to change

Key Takeaway
The accident's existence is not independent; it is the same as its existence in the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Everything you perceive—colors, sounds, knowledge—are accidents that have no independent existence. If your body is their subject, what remains of you if your body ceases to be? Does that thought change how you value these transient things?
?
The accident's existence is entirely dependent on its subject. Consider one accident you rely on daily (e.g., health, wealth, or status). If that accident were removed, would your sense of self be shaken? What does that reveal about what you consider your true essence?
?
You cannot point to an accident without pointing to its subject. In your life, what 'accidental' attributes (roles, possessions, relationships) do you treat as if they are you, when in reality they are not your essence? What would it mean to realign your identity with what truly exists not in a subject?

The Nature of Ḥulūl (Indwelling) and Its Types
Sanusi describes the conditions and types of ḥulūl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of ḥulūl (indwelling) as a complete meeting between existents, its conditions, and the distinction between ḥāl (state) and ṣūra (form).

Core Idea
Ḥulūl is a complete meeting where one existent's attribute is acquired by another, without physical contact.

Having defined the accident's mode of existence, Sanusi now provides a technical analysis of how this indwelling (ḥulūl) occurs. He distinguishes it from mere physical contact or adjacency, and introduces terms like ḥāl (state), maḥall (locus), and ṣūra (form) that are essential for understanding causation in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ḥulūl
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the negation conditions: not by way of mumassa (contact) or mujawara (adjacency), and no tabayun (separation) in position.
2
Understand the acquisition of attribute
The passage says the second existent acquires the attribute of the first. How does this happen? Consider the example of blackness and body.
3
Distinguish ḥāl and ṣūra
The final part explains that if the locus cannot subsist without the state, the state is called a form (ṣūra). What does this add to the understanding of accidents?

Inherence, Form, and Accident: Key Kalam Terms
Sanusi defines inherence and distinguishes form from accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of inherence (ḥulūl) in kalam and the distinction between a form and an accident.

Core Idea
Inherence is not like a body in space; form is essential, accident is not.

In this passage, al-Sanūsī explains a key technical concept: how an accident exists in its subject. He distinguishes this from the accidental presence of a body in space, and then further divides inhering states into those necessary for the subject's subsistence (form) and those that are not (accident). Understanding these terms is foundational for later discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of inherence
Read the passage's explanation that the accident's existence in itself is identical to its existence in the locus, such that pointing to one is pointing to the other.
Note the contrast with a body's existence in space.
2
Contrast with body in space
Notice how a body's existence in space is different because it can leave; it is not identical to its own existence.
3
Identify the two categories of inherence
The passage divides inhering states into form (essential) and accident (non-essential). Understand the criterion: whether the locus subsists without it.
Form is necessary for the locus; accident is not.
4
Reflect on why this matters for kalam
This distinction affects discussions on the attributes of Allah: are they forms or accidents? Kalam typically denies accidents in God but affirms essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وكذلك إذا لم يقيد مثل موجود لا في موضوع، فإن معناه ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا في موضوع وليس للواجب ماهية ووجود زائد عليها. ومعنى وجود العرض في المحل أن وجوده في نفسه هو وجوده في محله بحيث تكون : الأشارة الى أحدهما إشارة الى الآخر، بخلاف وجود الجسم في المكان فإنه أمر مغاير لوجوده في نفسه مرتب عليه زائل عنه ، عند الانتقال الى مكان آخر، فتحقيق ذلك أن ملاقاة موجود لموجود بالتمام لا على سبيل المماسة والمجاورة، بل بحيث لا يكون بينهما تباين في الوضع، ويحصل للثاني صفة الأول، كملاقاة السواد للجسم يسمى حلولاً ، والموجود الأول حالا، والثاني محلاً. والحال قد يكون بحيث لا يقوم ولا يتحصل المحل بدونه فيسمى صورة ومحلها مادة، وقد يكون بخلافة فتسمى الحال عرضاً والمحل موضوعاً.
They said: Likewise, if it is not qualified such as 'existent not in a subject', then its meaning is a quiddity which, when it exists, is not in a subject, and the necessary does not have a quiddity and existence additional to it. And the meaning of the existence of an accident in a locus is that its existence in itself is its existence in its locus, such that pointing to one is pointing to the other, unlike the existence of a body in space, for that is something different from its existence in itself, consequent upon it, and vanishing from it when moving to another place. So the verification of that is that the contact of one existent with another completely, not by way of touching or adjacency, but such that there is no separation in position between them, and the second acquires the attribute of the first—like the contact of blackness with a body—is called inherence, the first existent is called a state, and the second a locus. And the state may be such that the locus does not subsist nor is constituted without it, so it is called a form and its locus matter; and it may be otherwise, so the state is called an accident and the locus a subject.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of inherence (حلول) as the mode of existence of an accident in its locus, where the accident's existence is identical to its being in the locus, unlike a body's accidental presence in space. It then distinguishes two types of inhering entities: (1) the form (صورة), which is a state without which the locus (matter) cannot subsist, and (2) the accident (عرض), which is a state whose existence is not necessary for the subsistence of the locus (subject).

Correction: The passage clarifies that some inhering states are essential (forms) and some are non-essential (accidents). Forms are necessary for the subsistence of the locus (matter), whereas accidents are not.

D) A form can be separated from its matter.

Key Takeaway
Inherence is a relation where an inhering state's existence is identical to its existence in the locus; forms are essential (the locus cannot subsist without them), while accidents are non-essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson defines inherence as an inseparable identification between a state and its locus—such that pointing to one is pointing to the other. Is there any attribute of your own heart (like pride, envy, or gratitude) that has become so 'inherent' that pointing to you is pointing to that attribute? What would it cost you to uproot it?
?
If every accident in your life—from your health to your wealth—can vanish without altering your essential self, why do you cling to them as if they define you? How would your daily choices change if you truly lived knowing your essence before Allah is independent of all accidents?

The Term 'al-Hal' in Three Disciplines
Defines 'al-hal' in logic, kalam, and Sufism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'al-hal' is defined differently in logic, kalam, and Sufism, and why these distinctions matter.

Core Idea
The same word 'hal' carries distinct technical meanings in logic, theology, and Sufism.

This lexical note from al-Maqasid clarifies the term 'al-hal' (state) across three fields. Before delving into theological arguments, the author ensures the reader understands this foundational term's range of meanings. As we read, pay attention to how each discipline refines the concept for its own purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the general meaning
Start by reading the Arabic and translation to get a feel for the term's linguistic roots. Notice the general definitions before the technical ones.
2
Identify the three technical definitions
Look for the sections on logicians, mutakallimun, and salikin. Write down how each group defines 'hal' in one sentence.
The logicians: quickly-passing quality; mutakallimun: intermediate between existence and non-existence; Sufis: spiritual state that comes to the heart.
3
Reflect on the significance of the distinctions
Consider why each tradition needed its own definition and how these definitions relate to their broader concerns. How does the kalam definition differ from the Sufi one?
The misconception often is confusing the kalam and Sufi meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حال الشيء ضمنه وهيئته، وحال الدهر صروفه، وحال الإنسان ما كان عليه من خير أو شر، ولفظ الحال يذكر ويؤنث. ويطلق الحال على معان متقاربة كالكيفية والمقام والهيئة والصفة والصورة. ويقول المناطقة: الحال كيفية سريعة الزوال مثل الحرارة والبرودة واليبوسة والرطوبة العارضة. قال ابن سينا: بالفصول ينقسم الشيء إلى أنواعه وبالأعراض ينقسم إلى اختلاف حالاته. والحال في اصطلاح المتكلمين يطلق على ما هو وسط بين الموجود والمعدوم، وهو صفة لا موجودة بذاتها ولا معدومة لكنها قائمة بموجود كالعالمية النسبة بين العالم والمعلوم. والحال في اصطلاح السالكين هو ما يرد على القلب من طرب أو حزن أو بسط أو قبض، فالأحوال مواهب والمقامات مكاسب. الأولى تأتي من عين الجود والثانية تحصل ببذل المجهود.
The state of a thing is its immanence and configuration; and the state of time is its vicissitudes; and the state of man is what he is upon of good or evil; and the word 'hal' is used as masculine and feminine. And 'hal' is applied to similar meanings like quality, station, configuration, attribute, and form. The logicians say: 'hal' is a quickly-passing quality such as heat, cold, dryness, and wetness that occur. Ibn Sina said: By differentia, a thing is divided into its species; by accidents, it is divided into the differences of its states. And 'hal' in the terminology of the mutakallimun is applied to what is intermediate between existent and non-existent, an attribute that is neither existent in itself nor non-existent, but subsistent in an existent, like 'worldliness' which is a relation between the world and the known. And 'hal' in the terminology of the Sufis (salikin) is what descends upon the heart of joy, sorrow, expansion, or contraction; states are gifts and stations are acquisitions. The former come from the source of generosity, the latter are attained by exertion of effort.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This lexical note explains the word 'hal' (state) in three technical contexts. First, in general language, it refers to the condition or configuration of something. Then it lists its use in logic as a quickly-perishing quality. It quotes Ibn Sina's statement that things are divided into species by differentiae and into states by accidents. Second, in kalam (theology), 'hal' is a special technical term: an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent but inheres in an existent, such as the relation of 'worldliness'. Third, in Sufism, 'hal' is a spiritual state that comes to the heart, and it is distinguished from 'maqam' (station) by being a gift rather than an acquisition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun's 'hal' is the same as the Sufi spiritual state.
Correction: In kalam, 'hal' is a metaphysical category—an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent but subsists in an existent—not a spiritual experience. The Sufi 'hal' is a transient spiritual state that comes to the heart.

Checkpoint
In the terminology of the mutakallimun (theologians), what is 'al-hal'?
A) A quickly-passing quality.
B) A spiritual gift from God.
C) An attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent but subsists in an existent.
D) A divine attribute.

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-hal' takes on distinct technical meanings in logic, theology, and Sufism, reflecting each discipline's focus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The logician sees 'hal' as a fleeting quality—heat, cold, dryness. In your daily life, do you treat your spiritual states as passing moods or as intermediate realities that shape your relationship with the divine?
?
The kalam definition describes 'hal' as neither existent nor non-existent. Think of a time you felt a deep conviction about God that you could not fully articulate. Did you treat that conviction as real (existent) or dismiss it as unreal (non-existent)? How does this lesson challenge you to re-evaluate such experiences?

Classification of Accidents: The Nine Genera and Ibn Sina's Proof
Lists nine genera of accidents, critiques Ibn Sina's theoretical proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the classification of accidents into nine genera is based on induction, not demonstrative proof, and appreciate the critique of Ibn Sina's attempted rational division.

Core Idea
Accidents have nine genera, but the classification rests on induction, not proof.

This passage presents the standard classification of accidents into nine categories, but immediately emphasizes that this division is based on empirical induction (استقراء), not airtight demonstration. It then recounts Ibn Sina's elaborate attempt to rationally derive these categories, followed by a sharp critique pointing out logical flaws and circularity. Finally, an excuse is offered that such a division may still serve as a useful guide. As you read, note the tension between inductive and deductive methods in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation. Let the overall flow settle.
The passage is dense with technical terms; do not worry about every detail on first read.
2
Identify the nine genera
Locate the list of nine accident genera in the Arabic and English. Memorize them briefly: quantity, quality, place, time, position, possession, relation, action, passion.
These are the standard categories in Aristotelian/Islamic philosophy.
3
Note the basis of induction
Observe that the author states the classification is 'by the judgment of induction' and that proving it cannot be less or more is impossible. This is a key methodological point.
Induction here means empirical observation, not logical proof.
4
Trace Ibn Sina's rational division
Read the long conditional argument attributed to Ibn Sina. Notice how it divides accidents based on divisibility and relation. Follow the binary choices.
The division is supposed to derive the nine genera from first principles, but the critique will challenge its necessity.
5
Understand the critique and excuse
Identify the objections: incomplete disjunctions, non-necessary distinctions, and that the division is wasteful if it ultimately relies on induction. Then note the excuse: it can be a helpful guide.
The author is sympathetic to the critique but allows the division as a pedagogical tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجناس الأعراض بحكم الاستقراء تسعة، الكم، والكيف، والأين، والمتي، والوضع، والملك، والأضافة، وأن يفعل، وأن ينفعل. وعولوا في ذلك على الاستقراء، واعترفوا بأنه لا يمكن إثبات كونها ليست أقل ولا أكثر. وأن كل ما ذكر في بيان ذلك تكلف لا يخلو عن ضعف ورداءة (١) وإذا كان هذا كلام ابن سينا فلا وجه لما ذكر في المواقف من أنه احتج على هذا (٢) الحصر بأن العرض إن قبل القسمة لذاته فالكم، وإلا فإن لم يقتض النسبة لذاته فالكيف، وإن اقتضاها فالنسبة إما للأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض وهو الوضع، أو للمجموع إلى أمر خارج، وهو إن كان عرضاً فإما كم غير قار فمتى، أو قار ينتقل بانتقاله فالملك، أو لا فالأين، وإما نسبة فالمضاف، وإما كيف فالنسبة إليه إما بأن يحصل منه غيره فأن يفعل، أو يحصل هو من غيره فأن ينفعل، وإن كان جوهراً فهو لا يستحق النسبة إليه أو إليه إلا لعارض (٣) فيؤول إلى النسبة إلى العرض، ويندرج فيما ذكرنا، ثم اعتراضه بما في التقسيم من الترديدات الناقصة، والتعينات الغير اللازمة، وبأنه إن عول على الاستقراء كان هذا التقسيم ضائعاً، ولزمه الرجوع إلى الاستقراء من أول الأمر طرحاً لمؤونة هذه المقدمات، ثم اعتذاره بأنه إن أراد الإرشاد إلى وجه ضبط تسهل الاستقراء وتقلل الانتشار فلا بأس.
And the genera of accidents, by the judgment of induction, are nine: quantity, quality, place, time, position, possession, relation, action, passion. And they relied in that on induction, and they acknowledged that it is not possible to prove that they are not less nor more. And that everything mentioned in explaining that is forced and not free from weakness and poorness. And if this is the speech of Ibn Sina, then there is no basis for what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that he argued for this classification: that an accident, if it is divisible in itself, then it is quantity; otherwise, if it does not entail a relation in itself, then it is quality; and if it entails a relation, then the relation is either of parts to each other—that is position, or of the whole to an external thing, and if that external thing is an accident, then either it is a non-steady quantity—that is time, or a steady quantity that moves with its movement—that is possession, or not—that is place; and if it is a relation, then it is the correlate; and if it is a quality, then the relation to it is either such that something other than it results from it—that is action, or it results from something other—that is passion. And if it is a substance, then it does not deserve the relation to it or to it except accidentally, so it reduces to the relation to an accident and is included in what we mentioned. Then [the author of al-Mawaqif] objected to him due to what is in the division of incomplete disjunctions and non-necessary determinations, and because if he relied on induction, this division is wasted, and he must return to induction from the first, discarding the trouble of these premises. Then his excuse: if he intended guidance to a way of control that facilitates induction and reduces dispersion, then there is no harm.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of accidents into nine genera (quantity, quality, place, time, position, possession, relation, action, passion). It states that this classification is based on induction and that it is impossible to prove its exhaustiveness or minimality; any attempted proof is forced. It then recounts Ibn Sina's argument for this classification as presented in al-Mawaqif, which divides accidents based on divisibility, necessity of relation, etc. The passage then presents the objection that the division contains incomplete disjunctions and non-necessary distinctions, and that if it relies on induction, the elaborate division is unnecessary. Finally, an excuse is offered: if the purpose is to guide and facilitate induction, then it is acceptable.

Key Takeaway

Ibn Sina's Admission on the Impossibility of Proving the Limitation of Accidents
Ibn Sina admits the impossibility of proving the limitation of accidents and relies on induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's admission that the number of accidental categories cannot be proven demonstratively and relies on induction.

Core Idea
Even Ibn Sina admits the nine accidents are based on induction, not proof.

This passage begins with a remarkable admission: Ibn Sina himself acknowledged that it is impossible to prove that the accidental categories are exactly nine, and any attempt at such proof is contrived. Instead, the classification rests on empirical observation and induction. This sets the stage for a critique of later attempts at deductive proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the admission
Read the first sentence carefully: Ibn Sina admits that proving the number of accidental genera is impossible and that all such proofs are contrived.
2
Identify the nine genera
The passage lists the nine accidental genera. Note them: quantity, quality, where, when, position, possession, relation, action, passion.
These are standard in Aristotelian logic.
3
Understand the inductive basis
The text states that they relied on induction (istiqrā') for this enumeration, and admitted that induction cannot prove that they are not less or more.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this admission is significant: it means the classification is not a priori but empirical, and thus open to revision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد اعترف ابن سينا بأنه لا يمكن إثبات أنها ليست أقل أو أكثر وأن كل ما ذكر في بيان ذلك تكلف . وأجناس الأعراض بحكم الاستقراء تسعة، الكم، والكيف، والأين، والمتي، والوضع، والملك، والأضافة ، وأن يفعل، وأن ينفعل . وعولوا في ذلك على الاستقراء ، واعترفوا بأنه لا يمكن إثبات كونها ليست أقل ولا أكثر . وأن كل ما ذكر في بيان ذلك تكلف لا يخلو عن ضعف ورداءة . وإذا كان هذا كلام ابن سينا فلا وجه لما ذكر في المواقف من أنه أحتج علي هذا الحصر بأن العرض إن قبل القسمة لذاته فالكم، وإلا فإن لم يقتض النسبة لذاته فالكيف وإن اقتضاها فالنسبة إما للأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض وهو الوضع، أو للمجموع إلى أمر خارج ، وهو إن كان عرضاً فإما كم غير قار فمتي ، أو قار ينتقل بانتقاله فالملك، أولا فالأين وإما نسبة فالمضاف وإما كيف فالنسبة إليه إما بأن يحصل منه غيره فأن يفعل، أو يحصل هو من غيره فأن ينفعل ، وإن كان جوهراً فهو لا يستحق النسبة إليه، أو إلية إلا لعارض فيؤول الى النسبة الى العرض، ويندرج فيما ذكرنا ، ثم اعتراضه بما في التقسيم من الترديدات الناقصة، والتعينات الغير اللازمة، وبأنه إن عول على الاستقراء كان هذا التقسيم ضائعاً، ولزمه الرجوع الى الاستقراء من أول الأمر طرحاً لمؤ ونة هذه المقدمات، ثم اعتذاره بأنه، إن أراد الإرشاد الى وجه ضبط تسهل الاستقراء وتقلل الانتشار فلا بأس.
And he said: Ibn Sina has admitted that it is impossible to prove that they are not less or more, and that everything mentioned in explaining that is contrived. And the genera of accidents according to induction are nine: quantity, quality, where, when, position, possession, relation, action, and passion. And they relied in that on induction, and they admitted that it is impossible to prove that they are not less nor more. And that everything mentioned in explaining that is contrived, not free from weakness and poorness. And since this is the statement of Ibn Sina, there is no justification for what is mentioned in al-Mawāqif that he argued for this limitation by saying: accident, if it is divisible in itself, then it is quantity; otherwise, if it does not entail relation in itself, then it is quality; and if it entails relation, then the relation is either of parts to each other, which is position, or of the whole to an external matter; and if it is an accident, then either it is a non-permanent quantity, then it is when; or a permanent quantity that moves with its movement, then it is possession; or otherwise, then it is where; and if it is a relation, then it is relation; and if it is a quality, then the relation to it is either by something else originating from it, then it is action; or by it originating from something else, then it is passion; and if it is a substance, then it does not deserve relation to it or from it except by an accident, so it reverts to relation to the accident, and it is included in what we have mentioned. Then his objection to the division includes incomplete disjunctions and non-necessary determinations, and that if he relied on induction then this division is wasted, and he would be obliged to return to induction from the beginning, discarding the burden of these premises. Then his excuse: if he intended guidance to a way of systematization that facilitates induction and reduces diffusion, then there is no harm.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the enumeration of the accidental categories. It begins by stating Ibn Sina's admission that the limitation to nine accidental genera cannot be proven demonstratively and that all attempts at such proof are contrived. The text then notes that the nine genera (quantity, quality, where, when, position, possession, relation, action, passion) are accepted by induction (istiqrā'), and it is admitted that induction cannot prove they are neither more nor less. The author then criticizes a proof from al-Mawāqif that attempted a deductive division: if an accident is divisible in itself it is quantity; if not, then if it does not entail relation it is quality, otherwise it involves relation which is either position, when, possession, where, relation, action, or passion. The author points out the weaknesses: the division contains incomplete disjunctions and non-necessary determinations, and if one relies on induction, the division is pointless. Finally, an excuse is offered: the division might be merely a pedagogical tool to organize induction, which is acceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you rely on induction (examples or repeated observations) to form certain beliefs? Are you willing to admit, like Ibn Sina, that some of your convictions may lack absolute proof?
?
When you accept a classification or list learned from scholars, do you do so because it is rationally proven or because it is based on their authority and experience? How does this passage challenge you to examine the foundations of your own knowledge?
?
If even a philosopher of Ibn Sina's stature conceded the weakness of his own arguments, what does that mean for your own certainty when you present or defend Islamic beliefs? Will you humbly admit when your reasoning is based on induction rather than demonstration?
