The Deductive Proof from al-Mawāqif
The deductive argument from al-Mawāqif for the nine accidental categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deductive division of accidental categories as presented in al-Mawāqif and attributed to Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
A deductive attempt to derive the nine accidents from the nature of accident.

After noting Ibn Sina's admission, the author criticizes a specific proof from the work al-Mawāqif. This proof attempted a deductive division of accidents to arrive at the nine categories. The passage presents this argument in detail, showing its logical structure. Understanding this proof is essential for following the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the deductive division
Read the lengthy deductive division carefully. It starts with the condition 'if the accident is divisible in itself...' and branches out into multiple cases.
This is a classification tree.
2
Identify the decision points
The division uses a series of binary distinctions: divisible vs. not divisible, entails relation vs. does not, etc. Trace each branch.
3
Map the outcomes
Each branch leads to one or two accidental categories. List which categories appear at the leaves: quantity, quality, position, when, possession, where, relation, action, passion.
The division aims to cover all nine.
4
Reflect on the structure
Consider whether the division seems exhaustive. Are there missing possibilities? This will be the basis of the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد اعترف ابن سينا بأنه لا يمكن إثبات أنها ليست أقل أو أكثر وأن كل ما ذكر في بيان ذلك تكلف . وأجناس الأعراض بحكم الاستقراء تسعة، الكم، والكيف، والأين، والمتي، والوضع، والملك، والأضافة ، وأن يفعل، وأن ينفعل . وعولوا في ذلك على الاستقراء ، واعترفوا بأنه لا يمكن إثبات كونها ليست أقل ولا أكثر . وأن كل ما ذكر في بيان ذلك تكلف لا يخلو عن ضعف ورداءة . وإذا كان هذا كلام ابن سينا فلا وجه لما ذكر في المواقف من أنه أحتج علي هذا الحصر بأن العرض إن قبل القسمة لذاته فالكم، وإلا فإن لم يقتض النسبة لذاته فالكيف وإن اقتضاها فالنسبة إما للأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض وهو الوضع، أو للمجموع إلى أمر خارج ، وهو إن كان عرضاً فإما كم غير قار فمتي ، أو قار ينتقل بانتقاله فالملك، أولا فالأين وإما نسبة فالمضاف وإما كيف فالنسبة إليه إما بأن يحصل منه غيره فأن يفعل، أو يحصل هو من غيره فأن ينفعل ، وإن كان جوهراً فهو لا يستحق النسبة إليه، أو إلية إلا لعارض فيؤول الى النسبة الى العرض، ويندرج فيما ذكرنا ، ثم اعتراضه بما في التقسيم من الترديدات الناقصة، والتعينات الغير اللازمة، وبأنه إن عول على الاستقراء كان هذا التقسيم ضائعاً، ولزمه الرجوع الى الاستقراء من أول الأمر طرحاً لمؤ ونة هذه المقدمات، ثم اعتذاره بأنه، إن أراد الإرشاد الى وجه ضبط تسهل الاستقراء وتقلل الانتشار فلا بأس.
And he said: Ibn Sina has admitted that it is impossible to prove that they are not less or more, and that everything mentioned in explaining that is contrived. And the genera of accidents according to induction are nine: quantity, quality, where, when, position, possession, relation, action, and passion. And they relied in that on induction, and they admitted that it is impossible to prove that they are not less nor more. And that everything mentioned in explaining that is contrived, not free from weakness and poorness. And since this is the statement of Ibn Sina, there is no justification for what is mentioned in al-Mawāqif that he argued for this limitation by saying: accident, if it is divisible in itself, then it is quantity; otherwise, if it does not entail relation in itself, then it is quality; and if it entails relation, then the relation is either of parts to each other, which is position, or of the whole to an external matter; and if it is an accident, then either it is a non-permanent quantity, then it is when; or a permanent quantity that moves with its movement, then it is possession; or otherwise, then it is where; and if it is a relation, then it is relation; and if it is a quality, then the relation to it is either by something else originating from it, then it is action; or by it originating from something else, then it is passion; and if it is a substance, then it does not deserve relation to it or from it except by an accident, so it reverts to relation to the accident, and it is included in what we have mentioned. Then his objection to the division includes incomplete disjunctions and non-necessary determinations, and that if he relied on induction then this division is wasted, and he would be obliged to return to induction from the beginning, discarding the burden of these premises. Then his excuse: if he intended guidance to a way of systematization that facilitates induction and reduces diffusion, then there is no harm.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the enumeration of the accidental categories. It begins by stating Ibn Sina's admission that the limitation to nine accidental genera cannot be proven demonstratively and that all attempts at such proof are contrived. The text then notes that the nine genera (quantity, quality, where, when, position, possession, relation, action, passion) are accepted by induction (istiqrā'), and it is admitted that induction cannot prove they are neither more nor less. The author then criticizes a proof from al-Mawāqif that attempted a deductive division: if an accident is divisible in itself it is quantity; if not, then if it does not entail relation it is quality, otherwise it involves relation which is either position, when, possession, where, relation, action, or passion. The author points out the weaknesses: the division contains incomplete disjunctions and non-necessary determinations, and if one relies on induction, the division is pointless. Finally, an excuse is offered: the division might be merely a pedagogical tool to organize induction, which is acceptable.

Key Takeaway
The proof from al-Mawāqif attempts to derive the nine accidental categories through a complex deductive division, but it faces serious logical weaknesses.

Critique and Excuse for the Deductive Proof
Critique of the deductive division and a possible pedagogical excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the deductive proof and the possible excuse for its use.

Core Idea
The deductive proof is flawed; it may only be a pedagogical tool.

After presenting the deductive argument, the author launches a sharp critique. He points out its incomplete disjunctions and unnecessary distinctions. Moreover, if the basis is induction, the deductive effort is wasted. Finally, he notes a possible excuse: the division might be intended as a guiding tool to facilitate induction, not as a proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criticisms
The passage lists three criticisms: incomplete disjunctions (al-tardīdāt al-nāqiṣa), non-necessary distinctions, and redundancy if induction is used.
2
Understand the third criticism
If the division relies on induction, then the entire deductive exercise is superfluous; one should just use induction from the start.
3
Consider the excuse
The author mentions an excuse: the division might be intended only to organize and reduce the scope of induction, making it easier.
This excuses the division as heuristic.
4
Reflect on the overall message
Think about what this tells us about the reliability of such classifications in theology and philosophy.

The Ten Highest Genera: Substance and Nine Accidents
Philosophers classify possible beings into ten highest genera: substance and nine accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of the ten highest genera (al-muqūlāt al-‘ashr) for possible beings, including the distinction between substance and accident and the debates over the number of accidental genera.

Core Idea
Possible beings fall under ten highest genera: substance and nine accidents.

This passage introduces the philosophical classification of the highest genera (al-ajnās al-‘āliya) for all possible beings. The philosophers held that these are ten, with substance as the tenth and nine accidents. The passage also clarifies why 'the existent' is not a genus, and explains the essential nature of substance versus the accidental nature of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's presentation of the classification sink in.
Pay attention to the claim that each highest genus is a genus for what is under it.
2
Identify the main claim and disputes
Notice that the philosophers claim ten highest genera, but alternative views are mentioned: some say only three accidental genera, others add motion. Also, there is disagreement on whether 'whereness' belongs to 'where' or 'passion'.
These disputes show that the classification was debated.
3
Understand the basis: genus vs. common accident
The text emphasizes that each highest genus is a genus (jins) for what is under it, not a common accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm). The divisions under them are genera, not species.
4
Grasp the distinction between substance and accident
Substance is the essence and reality of a thing, essential; accident is what occurs to the subject after the realization of its essence. This is key to understanding the classification.
Accident is not essential for individuals but can be for shares within them, like walking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وزعموا أنها أجناس عالية عاشرها الجوهر، ويبتني على أن كلاً منها جنس وما تحته أجناس ، وليس الموجود جنساً للجوهر والعرض ولا العرض للأعراض، ولا النسبة للنسبيات، وقيل أجناس الأعراض ثلاثة : الكم والكيف والنسبة وزاد بعضهم الحركة . والجمهور على أن الأينية من الأين، وقيل من أن ينفعل كغير الأينية . قال وزعموا ذهب الجمهور من الحكماء إلى أن الأجناس العالية للممكنات عشرة وهي الأعراض التسعة والجوهر، ويسمونها المقولات العشر ومبنى ذلك على أن كلاً منها جنس لما تحته لا عرض عام وما تحته من الأقسام الأولية أجناس لا أنواع ، وليس الموجود جنساً للجوهر والعرض ولا العرض جنساً للأعراض التسعة ولا النسبة لأقسامها السبعة ، وبينوا ما يحتاج إليه من ذلك إلى البيان، بأن المعنى من الجوهر ذات الشيء وحقيقته، فيكون ذاتياً بخلاف العرض فإن معناه ما يعرض للموضوع ، وعروض الشيء للشيء إنما يكون بعد تحقق حقيقته، فلا يكون ذاتياً لما تحته من الأفراد وإن جاز أن يكون ذاتياً لما فيها من الحصص كالماشي لحصصه العارضة للحيوانات، وكذا النسبة.
He said: And they claimed that they are highest genera, the tenth of which is substance. And it is built on that each of them is a genus and what is under it are genera. And the existent is not a genus for substance and accident, nor accident for accidents, nor relation for relationals. And it was said the genera of accidents are three: quantity, quality, and relation, and some added motion. And the majority are of the opinion that the 'whereness' belongs to 'where', and it was said that it belongs to 'passion' like other than the 'whereness'. He said: And they claimed: The majority of philosophers went to the view that the highest genera for possible beings are ten, namely the nine accidents and substance, and they call them the ten categories. And the basis of that is that each of them is a genus for what is under it, not a common accident, and what is under it of primary divisions are genera, not species. And the existent is not a genus for substance and accident, nor accident a genus for the nine accidents, nor relation a genus for its seven divisions. And they explained what needs explanation of that: that the meaning of substance is the essence of the thing and its reality, so it is essential, contrary to accident, for its meaning is what occurs to the subject. And the occurrence of a thing to a thing is only after the realization of its reality, so it is not essential for the individuals under it, although it may be essential for the shares within them, like walking for walking's shares that occur to animals; and likewise relation.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage defining the ten highest genera (al-ajnās al-‘āliya) of possible beings. It begins by stating the claim that these genera are ten, with substance (jawhar) as the tenth. Each is a genus for what falls under it, and 'the existent' (al-mawjūd) is not a genus for substance and accident. An alternative view is mentioned: some say the genera of accidents are only three (quantity, quality, relation) with motion added by others. There is also a dispute on whether 'whereness' (al-ayniyya) belongs to 'where' (al-ayn) or to 'passion' (an yanfa‘il). The majority of philosophers (al-ḥukamā') hold that the highest genera for possible beings (al-mumkināt) are ten: nine accidents and substance, called the ten categories (al-maqūlāt al-‘ashr). The basis is that each is a genus for what is under it, not a common accident (lā ‘araḍ ‘āmm). The divisions under them are genera, not species. The existent is not genus for substance and accident; accident is not genus for the nine accidents; relation is not genus for its seven divisions. The explanation: substance means the essence (dhāt) and reality (ḥaqīqa) of a thing, so it is essential (dhātī); accident means what occurs to the subject, and occurrence is only after the realization of the essence, so accident is not essential for the individuals under it, though it may be essential for the shares (ḥiṣaṣ) within them, like 'walking' for the shares of walking that occur in animals. The same applies to relation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the ten categories apply to all existents, including the Necessary Existent (Allah).
Correction: The passage specifies that the ten categories are for possible beings (al-mumkināt) only, not for the Necessary Existent, who transcends categorization.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes substance from accident?
A) Substance is divisible, accident is not.
B) Substance is the essence and reality of a thing and is essential; accident is what occurs to the subject after the realization of its reality and is not essential.

D) Substance is one of the nine accidents.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' ten categories cover all possible beings, with substance as essential essence and accidents as attributes occurring to it after its realization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that substance is the essence of a thing, while accidents occur to it after its reality is realized. When you look at yourself, what do you consider your substance—your soul, your character—and what accidents—wealth, status, appearance—do you allow to define you? How often do you prioritize accidents over substance, and what will you change today to realign with your true essence?
?
The philosophers claim that the ten categories cover all possible beings, but the Necessary Existent falls outside them. Do you ever treat God as if He fits into one of these categories, limiting Him to a place, time, or attribute that you can grasp? In your daily life, when do you need to remind yourself that Allah is not under any category and is beyond all comparison?
?
The passage explains that 'existent' is not a genus for substance and accident—it is a common accident. Reflect: Have you ever thought of 'existence' as something that all things share equally, forgetting that the existence of the Creator is fundamentally different from that of creatures? How does this distinction affect your understanding of tawheed and your worship?

Defining Substance and Accident by Necessary Concomitant
Al-Taftazani argues that definitions of substance and accident are not essential but by necessary concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that the definitions of substance and accident as 'existent in a subject' are not essential but only by necessary concomitant.

Core Idea
Essential definitions capture the essence; necessary concomitants only describe a consequence.

Al-Taftazani begins his critique by pointing out that the common definitions of substance and accident are not true definitions. Instead, they are merely descriptions of a necessary concomitant that follows from the quiddity. This lesson examines the argument that existence is not an essential property and that these definitions are merely by necessary concomitant.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the source passage carefully, noting the structure and key terms.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement where al-Taftazani explicitly says these definitions are 'rasm bi al-lazim'.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Understand the argument about existence
Notice the series of conditional statements: if existence were essential, then certain consequences would follow, but they don't.
This supports why existence cannot be the essential property.
4
Distinguish between essence and necessary concomitant
Reflect on the meaning of 'rasm bi al-lazim' and how it differs from a true essential definition.
This is a key logical concept.
5
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للنسبيات السبع (١) فإنهم لا يعنون بها ما يدخل النسبة في ذواتها سوى الإضافة، فإنها نسبة مكررة على ما سيأتي الكلام في النسبة، وبأن الموجود لو كان ذاتياً لهما لما كان مقولاً بالتشكيك ولما أمكن تعقل شيء من الجواهر والأعراض مع الشك في وجوده، ولما احتاج اتصافه بالوجود إلى سبب كحيوانية الإنسان ولونية السواد، وتعريفهما بالموجود في موضوع والموجود لا في موضوع رسم باللازم ذلك فليس اللازم هو الوجود حتى يكون كل جوهر مثلاً موجوداً البتة، لأن معناه أنه ماهية إذا وجدت لم يكن في موضوع، وهذا المعنى هو اللازم له. وهذا مع ما فيه من ضعف مقدمات إثبات جنسية الجوهر ونفي جنسية العرض لا يفيد تمام المطلوب لجواز أن يكون للكل أو للبعض منها ذاتي مشترك هو الجنس، ولا معول سوى الاستقراء (٢).
As for the seven relatives (al-nisbiyyat al-sab‘), they do not mean by them what involves relation in their essences except al-idaafa (relation), for it is a repeated relation as will come in the discussion of relation. And by [the argument] that if existence were essential (dhati) to them (substance and accident), then it would not be predicated by analogy (bi al-tashkik) and it would not be possible to conceive any substance or accident while doubting its existence, and its being described by existence would not need a cause, like the animality of man and the blackness of black. And their definition as 'existent in a subject' and 'existent not in a subject' is a definition by necessary concomitant (rasm bi al-lazim); thus the necessary concomitant is not existence itself such that every substance, for example, exists absolutely. Because its meaning is 'a quiddity which, when it exists, is not in a subject', and this meaning is the necessary concomitant. And this, despite the weakness of the premises of proving the genus-hood (jinsiyya) of substance and negating the genus-hood of accident, does not achieve the full desired [proof] because it is possible that they (all or some) have a common essential (dhati) which is the genus. And there is no reliance except on induction (istiqra').
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a critical argument against defining substance and accident by 'existent in a subject' and 'existent not in a subject'. The author (al-Taftazani) explains that these definitions are mere concomitant descriptions (rasm bi al-lazim), not essential definitions. He supports this by noting that if existence were essential, it would not be predicated by analogy, and we could not conceive substances/accidents while doubting existence. Also, existence would not need a cause. The definitions actually state a necessary concomitant (lazim) that follows from the quiddity, not the quiddity itself. This approach fails to prove that all substances/accidents share no common genus, because they might have a common essential (dhati) that is the genus. The only reliable method is induction (istiqra'), which is incomplete. The passage guards against the mistake of treating concomitant properties as essential definitions.

Key Terms
رسم باللازم
ذاتي
تشكيك
النسبيات السبع
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definitions of substance as 'existent not in a subject' and accident as 'existent in a subject' capture their essence.
Correction: These definitions are merely descriptions of a necessary concomitant (rasm bi al-lazim), not essential definitions. Existence is not part of the essence of either.

Checkpoint
What reason does al-Taftazani give to show that existence is not an essential property of substance and accident?
A) Because existence is predicated univocally
B) Because we can conceive of substances and accidents while doubting their existence
C) Because existence needs a cause, unlike essential properties
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The definitions of substance and accident by 'existent in a subject' are not essential but are merely definitions by necessary concomitant, because existence is not an essential property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defined yourself or others by accidental attributes (like status, wealth, or appearance) rather than by your true essence? What would it mean for you to seek a deeper, essential understanding of yourself in light of this lesson?
?
The lesson shows that even well-known definitions can be incomplete and merely descriptive. When have you held a belief or understanding that you later realized was only a surface-level description? How will you guard against accepting mere necessary concomitants as the whole truth in your pursuit of knowledge?

The Weakness of Premises and the Need for Induction
Al-Taftazani criticizes the definitions as insufficient and highlights the need for induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani considers the definitions to be insufficient and why induction is the only remaining method, despite its limitations.

Core Idea
Definitions by necessary concomitant still fail to prove a common genus; only imperfect induction can help.

After establishing that the definitions are not essential, al-Taftazani further points out that even if we accept them as definitions by necessary concomitant, they do not achieve the desired goal of proving that substance and accident share no common genus. The premises for proving the genus-hood of substance and negating it for accident are weak. Moreover, it is possible that all or some substances and accidents have a common essential property that is their genus. The only way to determine this would be through exhaustive induction, which is not available.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second part of the passage carefully.
2
Identify the conclusion
Notice that al-Taftazani says the definition 'does not achieve the full desired [proof]'. What is the full desired proof?
It is to show that substance and accident have no common genus.
3
Understand the problem of a possible common genus
He points out that it is possible that all or some have a common essential (dhati) that is the genus. This undermines the definitions as complete.
4
Recognize the reliance on induction
The only reliable method is induction (istiqra'), but it is not exhaustive.
Think about why induction is not sufficient.
5
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding of the limitation.

And there is no reliance except on induction (istiqra').

The Genera of Accidents: Quantity, Quality, Relation, and Motion
Al-Taftazani presents opinions on the highest genera of accidents, including motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classifications of accidents into highest genera and the rationale for considering motion as a separate genus.

Core Idea
Accidents are classified into genera based on conceptual properties, with motion as a debated fourth.

In Islamic philosophy and kalam, accidents are categorized into the highest genera. The standard view recognizes three: quantity, quality, and relation. However, some scholars add motion as a fourth genus, defining it by the lack of conceivable stability. This passage from al-Taftazani presents these opinions and the subsequent discussion on the category of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage followed by the literal English translation to grasp the overall argument and terminology.
Notice the technical terms like 'ajnas al-a‘rāḍ' (genera of accidents).
2
Identify the two classifications
The passage presents two views: (1) three genera (quantity, quality, relation) based on division and relation; (2) adding motion as a fourth genus defined by inconceivable stability.
Compare the criteria for each genus.
3
Understand the definition of motion
Motion is defined as an accident whose stability is not conceivable in itself, distinguishing it from time, which is the measure of motion.
Note the phrase 'bi-dhātihi' (in itself) is crucial.
4
Examine the debate on motion's category
The passage discusses where motion belongs: the majority say motion in 'where' is from the category 'where', but some (like al-Razi) say it is from 'to be acted upon' because it is gradual change. A difficulty arises because motion is sensible while 'to be acted upon' is relational, leading some to exclude motion from categories altogether.
This debate shows the complexity of classifying motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعضهم إلى أن أجناس الأعراض ثلاثة: الكم والكيف والنسبة، لأنه إن قبل القسمة لذاته فكم، وإلا فإن اقتضى النسبة لذاته فنسبة، وإلا فكيف. وزاد بعضهم قسماً رابعاً هو الحركة، وقال: العرض إن لم يتصور ثباته لذاته فحركة، واحترز بقيد (٤) لذاته عن الزمان فإنه لا يتصور ثباته بسبب أنه مقدار الحركة، وإن تصور ثباته فنسبة، أو كم، أو كيف على ما مر. ثم الجمهور على أن الحركة في الأين من مقولة الأين، وقيل من مقولة أن ينفعل، لكونها عبارة عن التغير المندرج، وإليه مال الإمام الرازي. وأما الحركة في الكم والكيف والوضع (٥) فظاهر أنها ليست من الكم أو الكيف أو الوضع، فيتعين كونها من أن ينفعل، إلا أنه يشكل بأن الحركة موجودة ربما يدعي كونها محسوسة، وأن ينفعل اعتبارية، ومن ها هنا ذهب البعض إلى أن الحركة خارجة عن المقولات.
And some went to that the genera (ajnas) of accidents are three: quantity (al-kamm), quality (al-kayf), and relation (al-nisba). Because if it accepts division in itself, it is quantity; otherwise, if it necessitates relation in itself, it is relation; otherwise, it is quality. And some added a fourth genus, which is motion (al-haraka), and said: the accident, if its stability is not conceivable in itself, is motion; and he guarded by the condition 'in itself' against time, for its stability is not conceivable because it is the measure of motion. And if its stability is conceivable, then it is relation, or quantity, or quality as previously. Then the majority (jumhur) hold that motion in the category 'where' (al-ayn) belongs to the category of 'where'; and it was said it belongs to the category 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa‘il), because it is an expression of gradual change (al-taghayyur al-mundarij), and Imam al-Razi inclined to this. As for motion in quantity, quality, and position (al-wad‘), it is apparent that it is not from quantity, quality, or position, so it is determined to be from 'to be acted upon'. Except that it is problematic that motion is existent, perhaps claimed to be sensible, while 'to be acted upon' is relational (i‘tibariyya); and from here some went to that motion is outside the categories.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the highest genera (ajnas ‘aliya) of accidents. The author presents two opinions: first, that accidents have three genera (quantity, quality, relation) based on whether the accident accepts division by itself (quantity), necessitates relation (relation), or otherwise (quality). Second, some add a fourth genus: motion, defined as an accident whose stability is not conceivable in itself, distinguishing it from time. The author then discusses the category of motion, stating the majority view that motion in 'where' is from the category of 'where', but some (including al-Razi) say it is from the category 'to be acted upon' because it is gradual change. For motion in quantity, quality, and position, it must be from 'to be acted upon'. However, a difficulty arises: motion is sensible while 'to be acted upon' is a mental construct, leading some to exclude motion from the categories altogether. This passage details the internal debates on accident classification.

مقولة أن ينفعل

العرض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion always belongs to the category 'where'.
Correction: The text shows that while the majority hold that motion in 'where' is from 'where', some (including al-Razi) place it in 'to be acted upon', and others exclude it from categories entirely. So the category of motion is debated.

Checkpoint

A) Because motion accepts division by itself
B) Because motion necessitates relation
C) Because motion's stability is not conceivable in itself
D) Because motion is the measure of time

Key Takeaway
The classification of accidents into genera is not fixed; motion presents unique challenges that lead to multiple opinions on its categorization.

Are you obsessed with measuring motion (time) rather than seeking the timeless essence of your acts of worship? If time is just a shadow of change, what will you do today to connect with what never changes — Allah and His attributes?

Are Unity and the Point Among the Highest Genera? Two Answers
Al-Taftazani gives two answers to the objection about unity and point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether unity and the point belong to the ten Aristotelian categories, and the two main responses given by scholars.

Core Idea
Unity and point are not separate highest genera; they are either privative or under quality.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses an objection to the completeness of the ten categories: entities like unity and the point seem to fall outside them. He reports two answers that attempt to fit these concepts into the categorical framework—either as non-existent privations or as subcategories of quality. Let's examine these arguments and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Carefully read the Arabic text once to get a sense of the argument. Then review the literal translation to ensure you understand each claim.
Notice the structure: objection, then two answers, with a counter-objection to the first.
2
Identify the two answers
The passage gives two distinct answers to the objection. Find and label them: the privative answer and the quality answer.
The first answer is countered by an objection about the point being existent.
3
Analyze the logic of each answer
For each answer, consider its strength and weakness. Why might someone classify unity and point as privative? Why as quality?
The second answer seems less controversial, as it keeps them within the categories.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Think about why this debate matters for metaphysics and theology. How does classifying these concepts affect our understanding of reality?
This connects to the larger project of cataloging all possible existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوحدة والنقطة ليستا من الأجناس العالية (٢).
قال : وأما مثل الوحدة والنقطة .
فقيل : عدميتان كالجهل والعمي، وقيل من الكيف، وقيل خارجتان لكن لم يثبت جنسيتهما، والحصر إنما هو للأجناس العالية.
وكذا الوجود والوجوب والإمكان ونحوها (٣).
قال : : وإما مثل الوحدة والنقطة، لما حصروا المقولات في العشر المذكورة أن شيئاً بمعنى من الماهيات الممكنة التي تحيط بها العقول لا يخرج عنها بل تكون نفس إحداهما أو مندرجاً تحتها، ورد الأشكال بالوحدة والنقطة». فأجيب بوجوه:
الأول : أنهما من الأمور العدمية كالعمى والجهل والحصر إنما هو للأمور الوجودية. واعترض بأنه لو سلم ذلك في الوحدة فالنقطة وجودية لكونها ذات وضع على ما مر.
الثاني : أنهما من معقولة الكيف لأنهما عرض لا يقتضي قسمة ولا نسبة وهذا صادق عليهما.
Unity (al-wahda) and the point (al-nuqta) are not among the highest genera (al-ajnas al-‘aliya).
He said: As for such as unity and the point.
For it was said: they are privative (‘adamiyyan) like ignorance and blindness; and it was said they are from quality (al-kayf); and it was said they are outside (the categories) but their genus-hood has not been established, and the enumeration is only for the highest genera.
And likewise existence (al-wujud), necessity (al-wujub), contingency (al-imkan) and the like (3).
He said: And as for such as unity and the point, because they restricted the categories to the ten mentioned, that nothing in the sense of possible quiddities that minds encompass is excluded from them, but rather is either one of them or subsumed under them, and an objection was raised concerning unity and the point. So it was answered with aspects:
The first: that they are privative matters (umur ‘adamiyya) like blindness and ignorance, and the enumeration is only for existent matters. And it was objected that if that is granted for unity, the point is existent because it has position (dhat wad‘) as previously.
The second: that they are from the intelligible of quality (ma‘qula al-kayf) because they are an accident that does not necessitate division nor relation, and this applies to them.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that unity (al-wahda) and the point (al-nuqta) are not among the ten Aristotelian categories. The author reports that some say they are privative (non-existent), some say they are subsumed under quality, and some say they are outside but their genus-hood is unproven. The objection is answered with two aspects (wujuh) given here. The first answer claims unity and point are privative (like blindness), so they are not covered by the enumeration of existent categories. However, an objection is raised that the point is existent because it has position. The second answer classifies them under the category of quality, because they are accidents that do not necessitate division or relation. Note: the third answer is not present in this window. This passage shows the debate on whether certain entities fit the ten categories.

النقطة
الأجناس العالية
عدميتان
الكيف
معقولة الكيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think unity and point are separate categories or highest genera.
Correction: The passage argues they are either privative (non-existent) or subsumed under quality, thus not independent highest genera.

Checkpoint

C) Because they have position
D) Because they are outside the categories

Key Takeaway
Unity and the point are not separate highest genera; they are either classified as privative (non-existent) or as belonging to the category of quality.

The point (nuqta) is real because it has position. What in your life is real (like a sin or a duty) but you treat as if it were non-existent? Are you ready to acknowledge it and place it in its proper category?

The First Answer: Unity and Point as Non-Existent Matters
First answer and its objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first answer to the objection that unity and point are outside the categories: they are non-existent matters, and why the objection considers the point as existent.

Core Idea
Unity and point are non-existent, but the point has position, so it is existent.

The first answer to the objection argues that unity and point are non-existent matters like blindness and ignorance. However, a challenge is raised: even if unity is non-existent, a point has a position, making it existent. This lesson examines the claim and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to understand the flow.
The passage begins by stating that unity and point are not high genera, then presents the first answer.
2
Identify the claim
Find the statement that unity and point are from non-existent matters and that the enumeration only covers existent matters.
The commentator says both are non-existent like blindness and ignorance.
3
Examine the objection
Notice the objection: if that is granted for unity, the point is existent because it has position.
This objection shows the weakness of the first answer.
4
Reflect on the difficulty
Consider why the first answer fails for the point. How does position make it existent?
This sets up the need for the other answers.

الوحدة والنقطة ليستا من الأجناس العالية. قال: وأما مثل الوحدة والنقطة. فقيل: عدميتان كالجهل والعمي، وقيل من الكيف، وقيل: خارجتان لكن لم يثبت جنسيتهما، والحصر إنما هو للأجناس العالية. الأول: أنهما من الأمور العدمية كالعمى والجهل والحصر إنما هو للأمور الوجودية واعترض بأنه لو سلم ذلك في الوحدة فالنقطة وجودية لكونها ذات وضع على ما مر. الثاني: أنهما من معقولة الكيف لأنهما عرض لا يقتضي قسمة ولا نسبة وهذا صادق عليهما. واعترضوا بأنهم حصروا الكيف في أقسام أربعة هما خارجتان عنها. الثالث: إلزام أنهما خارجتان عن المقولات العشر ولا ذلك يقدح في الحصر لأن معناه أن الأجناس العالية لما تحيط به عقولنا من الماهيات المندرجة تحت الجنس هي هذه العشرة، وهذا لا ينافي وجود شيء لا يكون جنساً عالياً، ولا مندرجا تحت جنس عال، والإشكال إنما يرد لو ثبت كون كل من الوحدة والنقطة جنساً عالياً أو تحت جنس آخر، وبهذا يندفع ما قال الإمام الرازي لا بد في تمام الجواب من إقامة البرهان على أنهما من الطبائع النوعية دون الجنسية.
Unity and point are not among the high genera (al-ajnas al-'aliya). He said: As for the like of unity and point. So it was said: they are non-existent (adamiyyan) like ignorance and blindness, and it was said: from quality (al-kayf), and it was said: they are outside but their being genera is not established, and the enumeration is only for the high genera. First: both are from non-existent matters (al-umur al-'adamiyya) like blindness and ignorance, and the enumeration is only for existent matters, and it was objected that if that is granted for unity, the point is existent because it has position as previously. Second: both are from the intelligibles of quality (ma'qulat al-kayf) because they are accidents (arad) that do not necessitate division nor relation, and this applies to them. And they objected that they confined quality to four divisions while they are outside them. Third: asserting that they are outside the ten categories and that does not invalidate the enumeration because its meaning is that the high genera that our intellects encompass from the essences (al-mahiyyat) subsumed under a genus are these ten, and this does not negate the existence of something that is not a high genus nor subsumed under a high genus, and the objection only arises if it were established that each of unity and point is either a high genus or under another genus. And with this is refuted what Imam al-Razi said: 'There must be in the complete answer an establishment of proof that they are from specific natures (al-taba'i' al-naw'iyya), not generic ones (al-jinsiyya).'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a set of three answers to the objection that unity and point are outside the ten categories. The commentator begins by quoting the matn: 'As for the like of unity and point' and then summarizes three opinions: they are non-existent, from quality, or outside but not established as genera. The first detailed answer argues they are non-existent matters, but an objection notes that a point is existent because it has position. The second answer classifies them under the intelligibles of quality, but an objection counters that quality has only four divisions. The third answer asserts they are outside the categories yet not as high genera, so the enumeration remains valid because it only covers high genera. This third answer refutes Imam al-Razi's demand for proof that they are specific natures. The passage follows the commentary style of methodically addressing objections.

Key Terms
الوحدة (al-wahda)
النقطة (al-nuqta)
الأمور العدمية (al-umur al-'adamiyya)
الحصر (al-hasr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all non-existent things are equally not real, so the point is also non-existent.
Correction: The objection shows that the point has a position, which is a property of existent things, so it cannot be simply non-existent.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to the first answer regarding the point?
A) The point is non-existent like unity.
B) The point has position, so it is existent.
C) The point is from quality.
D) The point is a high genus.

Key Takeaway
The first answer fails because the point, having position, is considered existent, thus cannot be dismissed as non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief or practice in your Deen that you have dismissed as small or insignificant, like a 'point'. How does the objection—that a point has position and therefore existence—challenge you to reconsider its importance? Will you now give it its rightful place?
?
The first answer tried to classify unity and point as non-existent, but the objection showed that position implies existence. In your own life, what 'positions' or responsibilities have you ignored, thinking they were not real or important? How can you affirm their existence by acknowledging them?
?
This lesson shows that even a simple classification can have deep implications. Is there an area of your faith where you have accepted an easy answer without considering objections, like the first answer? Are you willing to re-examine it, even if it means overturning a comfortable assumption?

The Second Answer: Unity and Point from Quality
Second answer and its objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second answer that unity and point are from the intelligibles of quality, and the objection that they do not fit the four divisions of quality.

Core Idea
Unity and point are accidents of quality, but quality has only four divisions, which they don't fit.

The second answer tries to place unity and point under the category of quality, arguing they are accidents that neither imply division nor relation. However, critics object that quality is divided into exactly four types, and unity and point fall outside these four. This lesson explores this classification problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the second answer and its objection.
The passage includes the general background and then the second answer.
2
Understand the reasoning
Note why they are considered from quality: they are accidents that do not necessitate division or relation.
This definition of quality is crucial.
3
Identify the objection
The objection says quality has only four divisions, and unity and point are outside them.
The four divisions are well-known in Aristotelian categories.
4
Evaluate the strength
Consider whether this answer successfully resolves the problem or if it faces a similar difficulty.
This leads to the third answer.

The Third Answer: Outside But Not High Genera
Third answer and refutation of al-Razi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third answer that unity and point are outside the ten categories yet do not invalidate the enumeration, because the enumeration only covers high genera, and they are not high genera. This refutes al-Razi's objection.

Core Idea
Being outside the categories is fine because the enumeration only lists high genera, not every existent.

The third and most definitive answer asserts that unity and point are outside the ten categories, but this does not break the enumeration because the enumeration is only for high genera (al-ajnas al-ʿāliya). Since unity and point are not high genera, their exclusion is irrelevant. This answer also counters al-Razi's demand for a proof of their specific nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the third answer.
This is the longest passage, containing the refutation of al-Razi.
2
Understand the key distinction
Notice the distinction between 'high genera' and 'non-high genera'.
The enumeration only claims to cover high genera.
3
See how it refutes al-Razi
Al-Razi demanded a proof that unity and point are specific natures, but the commentator says the answer already suffices by showing they are not high genera.
No need for further proof of specific natures.
4
Reflect on the completeness
Consider why this answer is considered the most satisfying among the three.
It preserves the enumeration while allowing unity and point to be existent.

Existence, Necessity, and Possibility Outside the Categories
Commentary on existence, necessity, and possibility being outside the ten categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence, necessity, and possibility are not part of the ten Aristotelian categories and why this does not invalidate the enumeration of categories.

Core Idea
Existence is not a category—it is the ground of all categories.

The commentator here addresses a potential objection to the ten-category system: what about existence itself? Surely it cannot be a substance or an accident, yet it is the most universal thing. This passage explains that existence, along with necessity and possibility, are not high genera and thus do not break the enumeration.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the flow from statement to justification to objection and answer.
2
Identify the three items and their justifications
The passage discusses existence, necessity, and possibility. Notice why existence is neither substance nor accident, and why necessity and possibility are not quality.
Existence's uniqueness: everything depends on it.
3
Understand the Ibn Sina citation
Ibn Sina's view is that these are predicative meanings more general than categories and necessary for most essences—they are true species not under a genus.
This explains why they don't break the enumeration.
4
Grasp the objection and response
An objection says categories are for external realities while these are mental. The response: many categories (like relation) are also mental, so the objection fails.
This shows the consistency of the classification.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
Reflect on why this matters for kalam: establishing a precise vocabulary for metaphysical discussion.
Categories help define what can be said about created things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وكذا الوجود والوجوب والإمكان ونحوها، يعني أنها خارجة من المقولات العشر، أما الوجود فلأنه ليس بجوهر وهو ظاهر، ولا عرض لأن شأن العرض تقومه بالموضوع دون العكس، ومن المحال تقوم الشيء بدون الوجود، وأما مثل الوجوب والإمكان فلأنه ليس من الكيف لما فيه من معنى النسبة ولا من غيره وهو ظاهر، ومع ذلك فلا يقدح في الحصر لأنها ليست أجناساً عالية وهذا ما قاله ابن سينا وأشياعه أن المعاني المقولة التي هي أعم من هذه المعقولات ولازمة لأكثر الماهيات كالوجود والوجوب والإمكان والمعاني التي هي مبادئ وحدود لبعض الماهيات كالوحدة والنقطة والآن، فإنما هي أنواع حقيقية غير مندرجة تحت جنس، فلا يقدح فيما ذكرنا من الحصر. فإن قيل: الحصر إنما هو للحقائق الخارجية، وهذه اعتبارات عقلية فلا حاجة إلى ما ذكرتم: قلنا: كثير من المقولات ليست أعياناً خارجية كالإضافة، وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل.
He said: And likewise existence (al-wujud), necessity (al-wujub), and possibility (al-imkan) and the like – meaning they are outside the ten categories. As for existence, it is not a substance (jawhar), which is apparent, nor an accident (arad) because the property of an accident is its subsistence in a subject without the converse, and it is impossible for something to subsist without existence. As for necessity and possibility, they are not from quality (al-kayf) because of the meaning of relation (al-nisba) in them, nor from anything else, which is apparent. Yet this does not invalidate the enumeration because they are not high genera (al-ajnas al-'aliya). And this is what Ibn Sina and his followers said: that the predicative meanings (al-ma'ani al-maqula) which are more general than these intelligibles and necessary for most essences (al-mahiyyat), like existence, necessity, possibility, and the meanings which are principles and limits for some essences, like unity (al-wahda), point (al-nuqta), and now (al-an) – they are true species (anwa' haqiqiyya) not subsumed under a genus, so they do not invalidate what we mentioned of the enumeration. If it is said: The enumeration is only for external realities (al-haqa'iq al-kharijiyya), and these are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya), so there is no need for what you mentioned: We say: Many of the categories are not external entities (a'yan kharijiyya) like relation (al-idafa), and being acted upon (an yaf'al), and being acted upon (an yanfa'il).
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage explains that existence, necessity, and possibility are outside the ten categories but do not break the enumeration because they are not high genera. The commentator first justifies each: existence is neither substance nor accident because everything depends on it; necessity and possibility involve relation, so they are not quality. He then cites Ibn Sina's view that these are predicative meanings more general than the categories and necessary for most essences, making them true species not under a genus. An objection follows: the categories are for external realities, while these are mental considerations. The answer is that many categories, like relation and the two actions (being acted upon), are also not external entities, so the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الوجوب (al-wujub)
الإمكان (al-imkan)
المقولات (al-maqulat)
الجوهر (jawhar)
العرض (arad)
الأجناس العالية (al-ajnas al-'aliya)
ابن سينا (Ibn Sina)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is just another predicate or category like substance or quality.
Correction: Existence is not a category; it is a necessary concomitant of everything, more universal than any category.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that existence, necessity, and possibility do not break the enumeration of ten categories?
A) They are non-existent.
B) They are not high genera.
C) They are under quality.
D) They are substances.

Key Takeaway
The ten categories only include high genera; existence, necessity, and possibility are more general and thus outside the enumeration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is not a category but the ground of all categories, what does that mean for how you value your own life? Do you treat your existence as a constant gift from Allah, or do you take it for granted as a mere backdrop to your actions? What concrete habit could you adopt this week to remind yourself that you are sustained in existence at every moment?
?
The passage teaches that necessity and possibility involve relation—they point beyond themselves. In your own spiritual life, do you live with a felt sense of being contingent upon Allah, or do you fall into the illusion of self-sufficiency? How would your daily decisions change if you truly internalized that your existence is possible and that only Allah is necessary? What is one step you can take today to align your heart with this reality?

The Third Objection: Unity and Point Outside the Ten Categories
Refutation of the third objection by distinguishing highest genera from real types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that unity and point fall outside the ten categories does not undermine the completeness of the categorical enumeration, and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between highest genera and real types.

Core Idea
Not everything is a genus: some natures are genuine types outside the category hierarchy.

This passage addresses a sophisticated objection to the Aristotelian ten categories: if concepts like unity, point, existence, necessity, and possibility are not part of the categories, does that mean the enumeration is incomplete? The author explains that the categories cover all highest genera under which essences are subsumed, but some concepts are not genera at all—they are real types that exist outside the genus-species hierarchy. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines where the objection is presented: unity and point are outside the ten categories, which seems to break the completeness.
The objection is not fully spelled out; it is referenced.
2
Understand the refutation's core
The refutation states that the categories are the highest genera, and something not being a highest genus or under one does not invalidate the enumeration. The problem only arises if unity/point were genera themselves.
The key is the distinction between being a highest genus and being a specific nature.
3
Examine the extension to existence, necessity, possibility
The author shows that even existence is neither substance nor accident, and necessity/possibility are not qualities, yet they are not categories. They are still real but not genera.
This reinforces the author's argument.
4
Grasp Ibn Sina's solution
Ibn Sina classifies such concepts as real types not subsumed under a genus, and therefore they don't affect the enumeration.
This concludes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إلزام أنهما خارجتان عن المقولات العشر ولا ذلك يقدح في الحصر لأن معناه أن الأجناس العالية لما تحيط به عقولنا من الماهيات المندرجة تحت الجنس هي هذه العشرة، وهذا لا ينافي وجود شيء لا يكون جنساً عالياً، ولا مندرجا تحت جنس عال، والإشكال إنما يرد لو ثبت كون كل من الوحدة والنقطة جنساً عالياً أو تحت جنس آخر، وبهذا يندفع ما قال الإمام الرازي لا بد في تمام الجواب من إقامة البرهان على أنهما من الطبائع النوعية دون الجنسية قال : وكذا الوجود والوجوب والأمكان ونحوها، يعني أنها خارجة من المقولات العشر ، أما الوجود فلأنه ليس بجوهر وهو ظاهر، ولا عرض لأن شأن العرض تقومة بالموضوع دون العكس، ومن المحال تقوم الشيء بدون الوجود، وأما مثل الوجوب والأمكان فلأنه ليس من الكيف لما فيه من معنى النسبة ولا من غيره وهو ظاهر، ومع ذلك فلا يقدح في الحصر لأنها ليست أجناساً عالية وهذا ما قاله ابن سينا وأشياعه أن المعاني المقولة التي هي أعم من هذه المعقولات ولازمة لأكثر الماهيات كالوجود والوجوب والأمكان والمعاني التي هي مبادىء وحدود لبعض الماهيات كالوحدة والنقطة والآن، فإنما هي أنواع حقيقية غير مندرجة تحت جنس، فلا يقدح فيما ذكرنا من الحصر.
The third (objection): The necessity that they (i.e., unity and point) are outside the ten categories, and that does not detract from the enumeration, because its meaning is that the highest genera which our minds comprehend from the essences subsumed under a genus are these ten, and this does not preclude the existence of something that is neither a highest genus nor subsumed under a highest genus. The problem would only arise if it were established that each of unity and point is a highest genus or falls under another genus. And by this is refuted what Imam al-Razi said: 'One must, for a complete answer, provide proof that they are of the specific natures rather than generic.' He said: 'And likewise existence, necessity, possibility, and the like,' meaning that they are outside the ten categories. As for existence, it is not a substance (jawhar) – that is obvious – nor an accident (ʻaraḍ), because the nature of an accident is to inhere in a subject and not vice versa, and it is impossible for a thing to subsist without existence. As for necessity and possibility, they are not of the category of quality (kayf) because they contain the meaning of relation, nor of any other (category), and that is obvious. Nevertheless, that does not detract from the enumeration because they are not highest genera. And this is what Ibn Sina and his followers said: that the meaning-categories which are more general than these intelligibles and necessary for most essences, such as existence, necessity, possibility, and the meanings that are principles and limits for some essences, such as unity, point, and the present moment (al-ān), are indeed real types (anwāʻ ḥaqīqiyya) not subsumed under a genus; thus they do not detract from what we have mentioned of the enumeration.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its refutation concerning the ten categories. The objection claims that unity (al-waḥda) and point (al-nuqṭa) fall outside the ten categories, which would seem to break the completeness of the enumeration. The refutation explains that the ten categories are the highest genera that cover all essences we conceive; something can exist without being a highest genus or under one. Unity and point are not genera but specific natures (ṭabāʼiʻ nawʻiyya), so the enumeration stands. The refutation then addresses Imam al-Razi's point that one must prove they are specific natures, not genera. The author notes that even existence (al-wujūd), necessity (al-wujūb), and possibility (al-imkān) are outside the categories because existence is neither substance nor accident, and necessity/possibility are not quality (since they involve relation). Yet they do not break the count because they are not highest genera. Finally, the author cites Ibn Sina, who says that such concepts are real types (anwāʻ ḥaqīqiyya) not subsumed under a genus, so they do not affect the enumeration.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a concept is not a genus itself, it must be subsumed under a higher genus.
Correction: The passage shows that some concepts like unity, point, existence, necessity, and possibility are real types that are not subsumed under any genus, yet they are real. They exist outside the genus-species hierarchy, so the enumeration of highest genera is not affected.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the fact that existence, necessity, and possibility are not part of the ten categories not invalidate the enumeration?
A) Because they are non-existent.
B) Because they are merely mental considerations.
C) Because they are not highest genera.
D) Because they are accidents.

Key Takeaway
The enumeration of ten categories remains complete because only highest genera are counted; real types like unity, point, existence are not genera and thus do not break the count.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is not a category but a real type that underlies all things, then how do you sometimes treat the existence of Allah as if He fits into a human conceptual box? What would it mean to let go of that and recognize His existence as beyond all categories? Will you actively remind yourself of this when you feel you have 'figured out' Allah?
?
This lesson teaches that some truths (like unity and point) are not under any higher genus. Is there a truth about your own soul or your relationship with Allah that you have been trying to fit into a predefined category—like 'this is just a psychological phase' or 'this is a theological concept'—when in reality it is a unique, irreducible reality? What would it cost you to face that truth on its own terms?
?
The passage says existence, necessity, and possibility are not accidents or substances. Your own existence is not an accident; it is a given reality from the Necessary Existent. How does that change the way you view your own contingency and dependence on Allah? Are you living as if your existence is self-sufficient, or are you constantly aware that you subsist only through Him? What one change can you make today to embody this awareness?

The Accident Does Not Subsist by Itself: Three Refutations
Taftazani proves that accidents require a body and refutes three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that an accident cannot subsist by itself and the refutations of three objections: Abu al-Hudhayl's accidental will, the ancients' view on relations, and Abu Hashim's composition.

Core Idea
An accident must inhere in a single body; it cannot stand alone or in multiple loci.

Taftazani begins this investigation by affirming that necessity dictates an accident cannot subsist independently. He then systematically refutes three alternative views: Abu al-Hudhayl's claim of an accidental will without a locus, the ancient philosophers' notion that relations like proximity subsist in both relata, and Abu Hashim's argument that composition is a single accident subsisting in two parts. Each refutation reinforces the core kalam principle that accidents are dependent entities requiring a substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall flow, then the English translation. Notice how Taftazani structures his argument: a statement of necessity followed by three distinct objections and refutations.
The text ends abruptly; the argument is incomplete but the key points are clear.
2
Identify the three objections
Find the sections mentioning Abu al-Hudhayl, 'some ancients,' and Abu Hashim. For each, note what view they held about accidents and why Taftazani rejects it.
The refutations rely on logical necessity and avoiding absurdities.
3
Analyze the refutation of relations
Understand why the ancients' view on proximity and adjacency is refuted by distinguishing two accidents (like fatherhood and sonship) rather than one accident in two loci.
This clarifies that relations are not single accidents subsisting in two subjects.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
Note that the passage cuts off with examples like 'the unity of ten.' Consider what might come next—likely further refutations or a conclusion.
The missing text may be found in the following page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
العرض لا يقوم بنفسه
قال: الضرورة قاضية بأن العرض لا يقوم بنفسه، وتجويز أبي الهذيل بإرادة عرضية لا في محل مكابرة، وبأنه لا يقوم بأكثر من محل، وما ذكر من أنه لو جاز قيامه بمحلين لجاز اجتماع العلتين ووجود الجسم في مكانين، ولم يحصل الجزم بتغاير السوادين بيان الكمية، وتنبيه على مكان الضرورة وجوزه بعض القدماء زعما منهم أن مثل القرب والجوار من الإضافات المماثلة قائم بالطرفين، ورد بأن هناك عرضين يقوم كل منهما بطرف، كما في الأبوة والبنوة من الإضافات المتخالفة .
وأبو هاشم زعما منه أن تأليف أجزاء الجسم سبب لعسر انفكاكها، فهو صفة ثبوتية تقوم بجزأين لا بواحد، ضرورة ولا بأكثر وإلا لما بقى عند انعدام جزء وبقاء جزءين، ورد بمنع السببية ومنع بقاء التأليف الذي بين الثلاثة .
وأما مثل وحدة العشرة وتثليث المثلث وجوه البنية وقيام زيد، فليس
The Second Investigation: The accident does not subsist by itself. He said: Necessity judges that the accident does not subsist by itself, and Abu al-Hudhayl's allowing an accidental will not in a locus is obstinacy. And [necessity judges] that it does not subsist in more than one locus, and what was mentioned that if it were allowed to subsist in two loci, then it would allow the conjunction of two causes and the existence of a body in two places, and certainty about the distinctness of two blacknesses would not be obtained—[this is] an explanation of quantity and a warning about the place of necessity. And some ancients allowed it, supposing that things like proximity and adjacency are similar relations subsisting in both ends. It was refuted by [saying] that there are two accidents, each subsisting in one end, as in the case of fatherhood and sonship which are different relations. And Abu Hashim, supposing that the composition of the parts of a body is a cause of the difficulty of their separation, so it is a positive attribute subsisting in two parts, not in one—necessarily—and not in more, otherwise it would not remain when one part ceases and two parts remain. It was refuted by denying causality and denying the subsistence of the composition that is between three. And as for examples like the unity of ten, the triplicity of a triangle, the faces of a structure, and the standing of Zayd, it is not...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a proof that an accident ('araḍ) cannot subsist by itself, i.e., it must inhere in a body (jawhar). The author first states that necessity (ḍarūra) dictates this. He then refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that an accidental will can exist without a locus, calling it mere obstinacy. Next, he argues that an accident cannot subsist in more than one locus, because if it could, two causes (one for each locus) would combine, a body could exist in two places, and we could not be certain whether two blacknesses are distinct. This shows the impropriety of such a view. Some ancient philosophers allowed accidents like proximity (qurb) and adjacency (jīwār) to subsist in both relational ends simultaneously. The refutation is that these are actually two distinct accidents, each inhering in one end, like fatherhood (ubūwa) and sonship (bunūwa), which are different relations (iḍāfāt mukhtalifa). Then Abu Hashim (of the Mu'tazila) claimed that the composition (ta'līf) of parts of a body is a cause of their resistance to separation, so it is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya) subsisting in two parts necessarily (ḍarūra), and not in more, because if it subsisted in three, it would not survive the removal of one part while two remain. The author refutes this by denying the causality and also denying that the composition between three parts remains when one is removed. The passage ends with examples (unity of ten, etc.), but the text cuts off.

عرض
محل
إرادة عرضية
تأليف
إضافة
ضرورة
أبو الهذيل
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think accidents like relations subsist in both relata simultaneously.
Correction: The passage clarifies they are distinct accidents inhering in each relatum, like fatherhood and sonship.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against an accident subsisting in more than one locus?
A) It would allow two causes to combine and a body to be in two places.
B) It would make the accident eternal.
C) It would destroy the distinction between substance and accident.
D) It is simply impossible by necessity.

Key Takeaway
The kalam principle that accidents require a single locus is defended against three alternative views, each refuted by showing they lead to absurdities or misinterpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage dismisses Abu al-Hudhayl's view as 'obstinacy'. Have you ever clung to a belief about yourself or about God that, in your heart, you knew went against clear evidence? What would it take for you to submit to the truth, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
If every accident—every thought, emotion, or quality you experience—depends on a locus that itself does not subsist by itself, what does that say about your own self-sufficiency? How does this truth move you to depend entirely on the Self-Subsisting (al-Qayyūm)?

Summary: Accident Cannot Subsist by Itself or in Multiple Substrates
Opening summary of the second topic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core kalam doctrine that accidents cannot subsist independently and cannot occupy multiple substrates, along with a brief refutation of objections from relations.

Core Idea
Accidents depend entirely on a single substrate for existence.

This lesson introduces the second major topic of the chapter: the nature of accidents. The author states two foundational principles: an accident cannot subsist by itself, nor can it exist in more than one substrate. He immediately dismisses a deviant view and provides a concise argument using the example of relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
Read the first line: 'al-‘arḍ lā yaqūmu bi-nafsihi' – the accident does not subsist by itself. This is presented as a necessity.
2
Identify the two claims
Note that the passage makes two claims: no self-subsistence, and no subsistence in more than one substrate. Find both.
3
Examine the refutation of Abu al-Hudhayl
The author dismisses Abu al-Hudhayl's view of an accidental will not in a substrate as 'mukābara' (obstinacy). Understand why this is considered irrational.
4
Analyze the argument from relations
The author refutes the claim that relations like proximity subsist in two ends by stating there are two accidents, each in one end, similar to fatherhood and sonship.
This foreshadows a more detailed refutation later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
العرض لا يقوم بنفسه
(قال): الضرورة قاضية بأن العرض لا يقوم بنفسه، وتجويز أبي الهذيل بإرادة عرضية لا في محل مكابرة، وبأنه لا يقوم بأكثر من محل، وما ذكر من أنه لو جاز قيامه بمحلين لجاز اجتماع العلتين ووجود الجسم في مكانين، ولم يحصل الجزم بتغاير السوادين بيان الكمية، وتنبيه على مكان الضرورة وجوزه بعض القدماء زعما منهم أن مثل القرب والجوار من الإضافات المماثلة قائم بالطرفين، ورد بأن هناك عرضين يقوم كل منهما بطرف، كما في الأبوة والبنوة من الإضافات المتخالفة .
وأبو هاشم زعما منه أن تأليف أجزاء الجسم سبب لعسر انفكاكها، فهو صفة ثبوتية تقوم بجزأين لا بواحد ،ضرورة ولا بأكثر وإلا لما بقى عند انعدام جزء وبقاء جزءين، ورد بمنع السببية ومنع بقاء التأليف الذي بين الثلاثة .
وأما مثل وحدة العشرة وتثليث المثلث وجوه البنية وقيام زيد، فليس محل النزاع، وكأنه مراد أبي هاشم .
قال : المبحث الثاني : قد يكون من الضروريات ما يشتبه على بعض الأذهان فيورد في المطالب العالية، ونذكر في معرض الاستدلال ما ينبه على مكان الضرورة، أو يفيد بيان الكمية كامتناع قيام العرض بأكثر من محل واحد فإن الضرورة قاضية بأن العرض القائم بهذا المحل يمتنع أن يكون هو بعينه القائم بمحل آخر، إلا أنه بين كميته بأن تشخص ذلك العرض إنما هو بالمحل يعنى أن محله مستقل بتشخصه، فلو قام بمحلين لزم اجتماع العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد وهو تشخص ذلك العرض ونبه عليه بأن حصول العرض الواحد فى محلين كحصول الجسم الواحد في مكانين فلو جاز ذلك لزم جواز هذا وهو ضروري البطلان، وبأنه لو جاز قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين لما حصل الجزم بأن السواد القائم بهذا المحل غير السواد القائم بذلك، لجواز أن يكون سواداً واحداً قائماً بهما، واللازم باطل بالضرورة، وقد يكون منها ما لا يحتاج إلى التنبيه أيضاً ، كامتناع قيام العرض بنفسه، فالقول به كما نقل عن أبي الهذيل أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة عرضية حادثة لا في محل يكون مكابرة محضة، بخلاف قيام العرض الواحد بمحلين ولهذا جوزه بعض القدماء من المتكلمين الفلاسفة زعماً منهم أن القرب قائم بالمتقاربين والجوار بالمتجاورين والإخوة بالأخوين إلى غير ذلك من الإضافات المتحدة في الجانبين، بخلاف مثل الأبوة والبنوة، فإن قيام الأبوة بالأب، والبنوة بالابن. ورد بأنا لا نسلم أن الواحد بالشخص قائم بالطرفين بل القائم بكل منهما فرد مغاير للقائم بالآخر، غاية الأمر تماثلهما واتحادهما بالنوع ، ولا يلزم من اشتراك النوع اشتراك الشخص، وهذا كالإضافات المتخالفة مثل الأبوة والبنوة، فإن مغايرة القائم بهذا القائم بذاك في غاية الظهور، وجوزه ابو هاشم من المعتزلة زعما منه أن التأليف عرضي قائم بجوهرين ويمتنع قيامه بأكثر من جوهرين حتى إنه إذا ألف بين
The second topic: The accident does not subsist by itself. (He said): Necessity judges that the accident does not subsist by itself, and the allowance of Abu al-Hudhayl of an accidental will (irada 'aradiyya) not in a substrate is sheer obstinacy (mukabara), and that it does not subsist in more than one substrate. And what was mentioned that if its subsistence in two substrates were allowed, then the conjunction of two causes and the existence of a body in two places would be allowed, and certainty would not be obtained of the difference of the two blacknesses — [that] is an explanation of the modality (bayan al-kammiyya) and a reminder of the place of necessity. And some of the predecessors allowed it, claiming that like proximity (qurb) and adjacency (jiwar) among the similar relations (al-idafat al-mutamathila) subsist in both ends. It was refuted that there are two accidents, each subsisting in one end, as in fatherhood and sonship among the differing relations (al-idafat al-mutakhalifa). And Abu Hashim claimed that the composition (ta'lif) of the parts of the body is a cause for the difficulty of their separation, so it is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) subsisting in two parts, not one necessarily, nor in more, for then it would not remain when one part ceases and two parts remain. It was refuted by denying the causality and denying the remaining of the composition that was between the three. As for examples like the unity of ten, the triplicity of a triangle, the face of the building, and the standing of Zayd, they are not the subject of dispute, and it seems that is what Abu Hashim meant. He said: The second topic: Among necessary matters there may be what is obscure to some minds, so it is introduced in high demands, and we mention in the context of inference what reminds of the place of necessity or benefits the explanation of modality, such as the impossibility of the accident's subsistence in more than one substrate. Necessity judges that the accident subsisting in this substrate is prevented from being identical to the one subsisting in another substrate. But he explained its modality: that the individuation (tashakhkhus) of that accident is only through the substrate, meaning its substrate is independent in individuating it. If it subsisted in two substrates, then two independent causes would conjoin on one effect, which is the individuation of that accident. And he pointed out that the occurrence of one accident in two substrates is like the occurrence of one body in two places; if that were allowed, then this would be allowed, which is necessarily false. And that if the subsistence of one accident in two substrates were allowed, then certainty would not be obtained that the blackness subsisting in this substrate is other than the blackness subsisting in that one, because it might be one blackness subsisting in both. The consequent is false by necessity. And among them there is what does not need pointing out, like the impossibility of the accident's subsistence by itself. Thus, the statement about it, as reported from Abu al-Hudhayl that God is willing (murid) with an accidental will (irada 'aradiyya) occurring not in a substrate, is sheer obstinacy (mukabara mahda). This is unlike the subsistence of one accident in two substrates, and for this some of the predecessors among the mutakallimun philosophers allowed it, claiming that proximity subsists in the two things that are near, adjacency in the two adjacent, brotherhood in the two brothers, and so on from the relations that are unified (al-idafat al-muttahida) in both sides, contrary to like fatherhood and sonship, for fatherhood subsists in the father and sonship in the son. It was refuted: we do not grant that the one in person (al-wahid bi al-shakhs) subsists in both ends. Rather, what subsists in each is an individual (fard) different from what subsists in the other, their utmost being their likeness and unity in species. And the sharing of species does not necessitate the sharing of person. And this is like the differing relations such as fatherhood and sonship, for the difference between what subsists in this and what subsists in that is extremely apparent. And Abu Hashim of the Mu'tazila allowed it, claiming that composition (ta'lif) is an accident subsisting in two substances and its subsistence is prevented in more than two substances, so that if it composes between...
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a continuous explanation of the second topic (al-mabḥath al-thānī) concerning the nature of accidents (a‘rāḍ). The author begins by stating the central thesis: an accident does not subsist by itself, and it does not subsist in more than one substrate (maḥall). He immediately addresses a deviant view attributed to Abu al-Hudhayl, who claimed that God’s will is an accidental will not subsisting in a substrate—this is dismissed as sheer obstinacy (mukābara). The author then explains that the impossibility of an accident subsisting in multiple substrates is demonstrated by a number of arguments. First, if an accident could subsist in two substrates, it would entail the conjunction of two independent causes upon a single effect (the individuation of the accident), which is absurd. Second, it would be like a single body existing in two places, which is necessarily false. Third, if one blackness could exist in two substrates, we could never be certain that two instances of blackness are distinct, undermining all empirical knowledge. The author notes that some early thinkers (al-qudamā’) allowed an accident to subsist in two substrates using the example of relations (iḍāfāt) like proximity or brotherhood. The author refutes this by arguing that in such cases, there are actually two distinct accidents, each subsisting in one substrate, even though they are similar in species. He contrasts this with relations like fatherhood and sonship, where the difference is obvious. Next, Abu Hashim’s view on composition (ta’līf) is discussed: he claimed composition is an accident subsisting in exactly two parts of a body. The author refutes this by denying the causal claim and by noting that if composition subsisted only in two parts, it would vanish when a third part is added, which is not the case. Finally, the author clarifies that examples like the unity of ten or the triplicity of a triangle are not relevant to this dispute, as they are not accidents in the same sense. The passage thus systematically defends the kalam doctrine that accidents require a single substrate and cannot be self-subsistent.

Key Terms
العرض (accident)
المحل (substrate)
الضرورة (necessity)
المكابرة (obstinacy)
الإرادة العرضية (accidental will)
الإضافات (relations)

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that God's will is an accidental will not in a substrate?
A) It is a valid theological opinion.
B) It is sheer obstinacy (mukābara).
C) It is supported by necessity.
D) It is a subject of legitimate debate.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are wholly dependent on a single substrate and cannot subsist independently or in multiple substrates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage teaches that every accident depends entirely on a substrate; nothing in creation is self-sufficient. In what areas of your life do you act as if your own qualities—your strength, knowledge, or status—are self-subsistent and independent of Allah? What would it mean to truly internalize that every aspect of you is a dependent accident, sustained only by Him?
?
The author dismisses Abu al-Hudhayl's view as sheer obstinacy because it contradicts clear rational necessity. Is there a belief or attitude in your heart that, upon honest reflection, you know contradicts the clear truth of revelation or reason, yet you cling to it? Will you ask Allah to remove that stubbornness and grant you submission to the truth?
?
The lesson shows that even something as seemingly relational as proximity is actually two separate accidents. Consider your relationships with others: do you ever treat your connection to someone as if it were a single, self-subsistent bond that defines you, rather than a dependent accident that Allah creates and sustains? How should this affect your trust in Allah alone?
