Refuting Accident- Accident Subsistence
Refutation of arguments for accident-accident subsistence using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's systematic refutation of the claim that accidents can subsist in accidents, including their responses to specific examples and the clarification of unity and existence.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot subsist in accidents; alleged cases are non-existent, substances, relational, or mental.

This passage presents the theologians' response to a key objection: if a point can subsist in a line, and a line in a surface, then accidents can subsist in accidents. The mutakallimun systematically refute these examples and clarify that unity and existence are not accidents in the strict sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the overall argument. Note that this is a refutation of a false claim.
The passage is dense with multiple sub-arguments.
2
Identify the first two examples and their refutations
The objectors mention point/line/surface and smoothness/straightness. See how the theologians respond: either the terms refer to non-existent entities or they are substances, not accidents.
3
Understand the speed and slowness argument
The passage argues that speed and slowness are not additional accidents to motion. They are either intrinsic to the motion (due to interspersed rests) or relational considerations relative to another motion.
This is a key clarification in kalam physics.
4
Grasp the unity and existence discussion
The final part addresses unity and existence. The theologians explain that unity is a mental consideration (or even non-existent), and existence is identical to the essence in external reality, or mental, or intermediate. Thus, they are not accidents.
This connects to earlier discussions in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعرض وزعموا أن النقطة عرض قائم بالخط، والخط بالسطح، بمعنى أن ذا النقطة هو الخط، وذا الخط هو السطح، لا الجسم، ومن القائلين بجواز قيام العرض بالعرض من بالغ في ذلك وتمادى في الباطل، حتى زعم أن كلا من الوحدة والوجود عرض قائم بمحله. فتسمى فوحده العرض ووجوده يكون من قيام العرض بالعرض. وأجاب المتكلمون بأن مثل النقطة والخط عدمي، ولو سلم فمن الجواهر لا الأعراض على ما سيجيء، ومثل الملاسة والاستقامة على تقدير كونه وجودياً إنما يقوم بالجسم، وبأن السرعة والبطء ليس عرضاً زائداً على الحركة قائماً بها، بل الحركة أمر ممتد يتخلله سكنات أقل، أو أكثر باعتبارها سريعة أو بطيئة، ولو سلم أن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات فطبقات الحركات أنواع مختلفة والسرعة والبطء عائد إلى الذاتيات دون العرضيات أو هما من الاعتبارات اللاحقة للحركة بحسب الإضافة إلى حركة أخرى بقطع المسافة المعينة في زمان أقل أو أكثر، ولهذا يختلف باختلاف الإضافة فتكون السريعة بطيئة بالنسبة إلى الأسرع وبالجملة فليس هناك عرضي هو الحركة، وآخر هو السرعة أو البطء، وأما الوحدة والوجود فقد سبق أن الوحدة اعتبار عقلي بل عدمي وأن الوجود في الخارج نفس الماهية، أو هو من الاعتبارات العقلية أو واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، وفي الجملة فجعله من قبيل الأعراض خطأ فاحش لا ينبغي أن يقول به المحصل فإن من شأن العرض أن يفتقر في التقوم إلى المحل ويستغني عنه المحل.
By accident, they claimed that the point is an accident subsisting in the line, and the line in the surface, meaning that the possessor of the point is the line, and the possessor of the line is the surface, not the body. And among those who assert the permissibility of the subsistence of an accident in an accident are those who exaggerated in that and persisted in falsehood, until they claimed that each of unity and existence is an accident subsisting in its substrate. So they named it: the accident's unity and existence arise from the subsistence of an accident in an accident. The mutakallimun replied that such as the point and line are non-existent; and even if granted, they are from substances not accidents, as will come. And such as smoothness and straightness, if they are considered existent, they only subsist in the body. And that speed and slowness are not an accident additional to motion subsisting in it; rather, motion is an extended matter interspersed with fewer or more rests, being fast or slow by virtue of them. Even if one grants that slowness is not due to interspersed rests, the layers of motions are different types, and speed and slowness revert to the intrinsic rather than the accidental, or they are among the considerations attached to motion according to its relation to another motion by traversing the specified distance in less or more time. Hence, they differ by variation of relation, so the fast becomes slow relative to the faster. In sum, there is no accidental that is motion and another that is speed or slowness. As for unity and existence, it has preceded that unity is a mental consideration, nay non-existent, and that existence externally is the essence itself, or it is among mental considerations or an intermediary between existent and non-existent. In sum, making it a category of accidents is a gross error which the thorough scholar should not utter, for the nature of an accident is to depend on a substrate for its subsistence while the substrate is independent of it.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors claim that a point is an accident subsisting in a line, a line in a surface, etc., as evidence that accidents can subsist in accidents. They exaggerate by asserting that unity and existence are accidents subsisting in their substrates. The mutakallimun answer: first, point and line are non-existent (عدميان); and even if granted, they are substances, not accidents. Second, smoothness and straightness, if existent, subsist directly in the body, not in another accident. Third, speed and slowness are not additional accidents to motion; motion is extended with interspersed rests making it fast or slow. Even if one denies rests, the various types of motion differ essentially, and speed/slowness are either intrinsic attributes or relational considerations to another motion. Fourth, unity is a mental consideration (اعتبار عقلي) or non-existent, and existence is identical to essence externally or mental or an intermediary. Thus classifying them as accidents is a gross error; an accident by nature depends on a substrate while the substrate does not depend on it.

جوهر
عدم
حركة
سرعة
بطء
وحدة
وجود
اعتبار عقلي
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The examples (point in line, speed in motion) prove that accidents can subsist in accidents.
Correction: The theologians show that these examples are either non-existent entities, substances, or relational attributes, not accidents inhering in accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun in this passage, how do they explain the relationship between speed and motion?
A) Speed is a separate accident subsisting in motion.
B) Speed and slowness are additional accidental attributes.
C) Speed and slowness are relations or intrinsic to motion, not separate accidents.
D) Speed is the motion itself without any distinction.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun argue that all claimed instances of accidents subsisting in accidents are either non-existent, substances, relational, or mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the theologians considered many supposed attributes (like speed as a separate entity) to be mental constructs or relations. How much of what you think is 'real' about your own character or your relationship with Allah is actually a mental construct that needs to be re-examined? Will you commit to testing your assumptions against the Qur'an and Sunnah today?
?
The source warns that making existence an accident is a 'gross error.' Reflect on your understanding of Allah's attributes: are you ever tempted to think of them as separate 'accidents' added to His essence? How can you purify your belief to truly affirm that His attributes are neither identical to nor separate from His essence, as the Ahl al-Sunnah teach?
?
The mutakallimun took great care to refute each false claim about accidents. What is one comfortable belief or habit in your life that you have never critically examined? Will you take the time to study it with the same rigor, even if it challenges you?

Accidents Do Not Last: Two Classical Proofs
Two arguments against accident endurance with rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical arguments against the endurance of accidents: the definitional reasoning of the externalists and the two logical proofs of the verifiers, along with their rebuttals.

Core Idea
Accidents are momentary by nature; their endurance would lead to logical paradoxes.

This passage tackles a foundational doctrine in kalam: that accidents cannot persist for two consecutive moments. It presents two distinct lines of reasoning — one based on the very definition of what an accident is, and another that explores the logical consequences if accidents were to endure. As you read, pay attention to how the arguments are structured and how objections are anticipated.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation once without pausing. Get a sense of the overall flow: the classification of proponents and their arguments.
Notice the two groups: externalists (الظاهريون) and verifiers (المحققون).
2
Analyze the externalists’ argument
Identify why the externalists think accidents cannot endure by definition. Consider their two sub-arguments and why they are considered weak.
They argue that endurance is incompatible with the concept of an accident.
3
Examine the verifiers’ first proof
Understand the claim that an enduring accident would require an accident to subsist in another accident, which is deemed impossible. Note the rebuttal.
The rebuttal is that both premises can be denied.
4
Examine the verifiers’ second proof
This is a more complex argument: if an accident endured, it could never cease. Follow the five ways an enduring thing might cease and why each fails.
The five ways: by itself, by removal of condition, by a contrary, by an agent, or by annihilation of substrate.
5
Consider the objection and response
The analogy with bodies raises a challenge. See how the response distinguishes between accidents and bodies.
Bodies endure but they are removed by divine action, not by inherent change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس الأعراض لا تبقى زمانين قال: ذهب كثير من المتكلمين إلى امتناع بقاء العرض، فالظاهريون لأن استحالة البقاء معتبرة في مفهوم هذا الاسم كالعارض ونحوه ولأنه لو بقي فإما ببقاء محله فيدوم بدوامه ويتصف بسائر صفاته وإما ببقاء آخر فيمكن بقاؤه مع فناء المحل وضعفهما ظاهر والمحققون لوجهين: الأول: أنه لو كان باقياً يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، وهو محال ورد بمنع المقدمتين. الثاني: لو بقي لامتنع زواله إذ لو أمكن فإما بنفسه فيمتنع وجوده، أو بزوال شرط فيتسلسل أو بطريان ضد فيدور لأن اتصاف المحل بأحد الضدين مشروط بانتفاء الآخر على أن زوال الباقي بالطارىء ليس أولى، بل بالعكس لأن الدفع أهون من الرفع أو بفاعل فيقتضي أثراً والنفي المحض لا يصلح، ورد أولاً بالنقض بالجسم، وقد يدفع بأنه يزول بأن يخلق الله تعالى.
The fifth investigation: Accidents do not last for two moments. He said: Many of the mutakallimun have gone to the impossibility of the endurance of an accident. The externalists (al-zahiriyun) because the impossibility of endurance is considered in the concept of this name, like 'al-‘arid' and the like, and because if it lasted, then either by the endurance of its substrate, so it would continue by its continuation and be qualified by all its attributes, or by another endurance, so it could possibly continue with the annihilation of the substrate—and the weakness of both is apparent. The verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) state two proofs: First: that if it were enduring, the subsistence of an accident in an accident would necessarily follow, which is impossible, and it is rebutted by denying the two premises. Second: if it lasted, its removal would be impossible; for if removal were possible, then either by itself—so its existence would be impossible—or by removal of a condition—so that would lead to infinite regress—or by the occurrence of a contrary (did)—so that would lead to circularity, because the qualification of the substrate by one of the two contraries is conditional on the absence of the other, while the removal of the enduring by the occurring contrary is not more appropriate; rather, the opposite, because prevention is easier than removal—or by an agent, and that would require an effect, and pure negation is not suitable. It is first rebutted by the objection of bodies, and it may be repelled by saying that it is removed by God creating.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument. It begins the fifth investigation (المبحث الخامس) on the impossibility of accidents lasting for two moments. It classifies the proponents: many mutakallimun hold this view. The externalists (الظاهريون) argue from the definition: 'accident' (عارض) implies non-endurance, and if it lasted, it would either last with its substrate (so it would be eternal as the substrate is eternal? Actually it says if it lasts by its substrate's endurance, then it would continue as long as the substrate continues, and be qualified by its attributes; or if by another endurance, it could outlast the substrate—both are weak. The verifiers (المحققون) give two proofs: First, if an accident endured, it would entail an accident subsisting in an accident (قيام العرض بالعرض), which is impossible. This proof is met by denying both premises (منع المقدمتين). Second, if it endured, its removal would be impossible. The argument then enumerates the possible ways an enduring accident could cease: by itself (impossible, as it would have to not exist while enduring), by removal of a condition (leads to infinite regress), by a contrary (circularity because contraries are conditioned on absence of each other, and removal by the occurring contrary is not more justified than the opposite), or by an agent (requires an effect, but pure negation is not a suitable effect). An objection is raised by analogy to bodies (which endure but are removed); the response is that removal of bodies occurs by God creating something causing their removal.

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
زوال
قيام العرض بالعرض
ضد
فاعل
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of removal argument is a simple logical point that dismisses the endurance of accidents without nuance.
Correction: The argument is actually a detailed analysis of possible modes of removal (by itself, by condition, by contrary, by agent) and shows the problems inherent in each, but it also acknowledges objections and responses.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof of the verifiers (المحققون) against the endurance of accidents?
A) If accidents lasted, they would be eternal.
B) If accidents lasted, they would require an accident to subsist in an accident.
C) If accidents lasted, they could not be created.
D) If accidents lasted, they would be substances.

Key Takeaway
The kalam denial of endurance for accidents is defended by both definitional and logical arguments, but each argument invites nuanced objections and responses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident in your life—your health, wealth, and emotions—can end in a moment, and only God can cause its removal or continuation, how does that change the way you cling to these things? What specific attachment do you need to release today?
?
The argument shows that prevention is easier than removal (al-daf' ahwan min al-raf'). Where in your own spiritual life are you waiting for removal of a bad habit when you could have prevented it? What would prevention look like starting now?
?
The verifiers' arguments force you to confront the logical impossibility of anything enduring without God's constant creation. If you truly believed that every atom of your being is being recreated by God every moment, how would you differ in your prayer, gratitude, and fear of Him? Commit to one change tonight.

Accidents Do Not Persist: Position and Refutation
Taftazani states the position that accidents do not persist and refutes two arguments from ahl al-zahir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological position that accidents do not persist, the two arguments of ahl al-ẓāhir against accident persistence, and the refutation of these arguments based on linguistic and logical analysis.

Core Idea
Accidents are continually renewed by God; their linguistic meaning does not prove non-persistence.

This passage presents the Ash'ari position that accidents do not endure for two moments but are continuously recreated by God. It then introduces two arguments from those who take the outward meaning of language (ahl al-ẓāhir) to argue that accidents cannot persist. Finally, it refutes these arguments by distinguishing linguistic meaning from technical terminology and by clarifying the conditional nature of accident persistence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the position
Read the opening statement that accidents do not persist and what is meant by their 'permanence'—that it is the renewal of similar accidents by God's will.
Note the connection between accident persistence and the need for a sustaining cause.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the two arguments presented by ahl al-ẓāhir: the linguistic derivation of 'accident' implying non-persistence, and the logical dilemma of persistence requiring either the persistence of the locus or another persistence.
3
Examine the refutation
See how the author counters each argument: first by distinguishing linguistic meaning from technical meaning, second by showing that persistence by another does not entail independence from the locus.
Focus on the conditional nature of accident existence and persistence.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this distinction matters for the broader kalām framework of causality and divine action—accidents are entirely dependent on God's continuous creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: ذهب كثير من المتكلمين إلى أن شيئاً من الأعراض لا يبقى زمانين بل كلها على التقضي والتجدد كالحركة والزمان عند الفلاسفة، وبقاؤها عبارة عن تجدد الأمثال بإرادة الله تعالى . وبقاء الجوهر مشروط بالعرض، فمن هاهنا يحتاجان في بقائهما إلى المؤثر، مع أن علة الاحتياج هي الحدوث لا الإمكان. احتج أهل الظاهر منهم بوجهين . أن العرض اسم لما يمتنع بقاؤه، بدلالة مأخذ الاشتقاق). يقال عرض لفلان أمر أي معنى لا قرار له وهذا أمر عارض، وهذه الحالة ليست بأصيلة، بل عارضة، ولهذا يسمى السحاب عارضاً، وليس اسماً لما لا يقوم بذاته، بل يفتقر إلى محل يقوم به، إذ ليس في معناه اللغوي ما ينبيء عن هذا المعنى . الثاني، أنه لو بقي فإما ببقاء محله فيلزم أن يدوم بدوامه لأن الدوام هو البقاء، وأن يتصف بسائر صفاته من التحيز والتقوم بالذات، وغير ذلك لكونها من توابع البقاء. وإما ببقاء آخر فيلزم أن يمكن فناء المحل ضرورة أنه لا تعلق لبقائه ببقائه، وكلا الوجهين في غاية الضعف، لأن العروض في اللغة إنما ينبيء عن عدم الدوام لا عن عدم البقاء في زمانين وأكثر ، ولو سلم فلا يلزم في المعنى المصطلح عليه اعتبار هذا المعنى بالكلية، ولأن بقاءه ببقاء آخر لا يستلزم إمكان بقائه مع فناء المحل الجواز أن يكون بقاؤه مشروطاً ببقاء المحل كوجوده بوجوده.
The fifth investigation: Many theologians have held that no accident persists for two moments; rather, all are subject to passing away and renewal, like motion and time according to the philosophers, and their permanence consists in the renewal of their likes by the will of God Almighty. The permanence of a substance is conditional upon the accident, so from this they both need the effecting agent in their permanence, even though the cause of need is originatedness (al-ḥudūth), not possibility (al-imkān). The people of outward meaning (ahl al-ẓāhir) among them argued with two proofs. [First:] That 'accident' is a name for what is impossible to persist, by the indication of the derivation (al-ishtiqāq). It is said: 'an accident (ʿaraḍ) occurred to so-and-so,' meaning a meaning that has no stability, and this is a contingent matter, and this state is not original but contingent, and for this reason a cloud is called 'accidental' (ʿāriḍ), [but accident] is not a name for what does not subsist by itself, but rather is in need of a locus in which it subsists, since in its linguistic meaning there is nothing indicating this meaning. Second: If it were to persist, then either by the persistence of its locus, in which case it would be necessary that it endure with its endurance because endurance is persistence, and that it be qualified by its other attributes, such as occupying space and subsisting by itself, and others, because they are among the concomitants of persistence; or by another persistence, in which case it would be necessary that the annihilation of the locus be possible, because its persistence has no connection with its persistence. Both aspects are extremely weak, because 'accidentalness' (al-ʿurūḍ) in language indicates only lack of duration, not lack of persistence for two or more moments; and even if granted, this meaning is not necessarily considered in the technical sense at all; and because its persistence by another persistence does not entail the possibility of its persistence with the annihilation of the locus, for it is possible that its persistence be conditional upon the persistence of the locus, just as its existence is conditional upon its existence.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an investigative section (mabḥath) in which the author presents the theological position that accidents do not persist for two moments; rather, they are continually renewed, akin to how philosophers conceive of motion and time. Permanence of an accident is just the creation of similar accidents by God. This view implies that the persistence of a substance depends on accidents, so both need a sustaining cause, and the reason for need is originatedness (ḥudūth), not mere possibility (imkān). Then the author reports two arguments from ahl al-ẓāhir (those who take the outward meaning of terms) to prove that accidents cannot persist. 

First argument: The very name 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) in Arabic derivation implies something that does not last, as in the phrase 'an accident occurred' meaning a non-stable matter. They argue that an accident is inherently contingent and non-enduring. But the author refutes this by pointing out that the linguistic meaning of 'accidentalness' indicates lack of duration, not lack of persistence across multiple moments; and even if it did, the technical theological definition does not need to follow that linguistic sense entirely. 

Second argument: If an accident were to persist, it would require either the persistence of its locus (leading to it inheriting all of the locus's enduring attributes) or another persistence (which would make the annihilation of the locus possible). The author dismisses both as weak: the first does not follow necessarily, and the second ignores the possibility that the accident's persistence is conditional upon the persistence of its locus, just as its existence was conditional. Thus both arguments fail to prove the impossibility of persistence.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
بقاء (persistence)
حدوث (originatedness)
إمكان (possibility)
أهل الظاهر (ahl al-zahir)
اشتقاق (derivation)
عارض (contingent/accidental)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The linguistic meaning of 'accident' (عرض) directly determines its technical theological meaning, so accidents cannot persist because the word implies instability.
Correction: The linguistic meaning only indicates lack of duration, not lack of persistence across multiple moments. The technical definition is broader and not necessarily tied to the linguistic implication of non-persistence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what mistake do the two arguments of ahl al-ẓāhir make regarding the concept of 'accident'?
A) They confuse linguistic meaning with technical meaning and assume that persistence by another entails possibility of survival without the locus.
B) They correctly prove that accidents cannot persist for two moments.
C) They rely on the philosophers' view of time and motion.
D) They assume that the existence of accidents requires a sustaining cause.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are not self-persisting but are continuously renewed by God, and linguistic etymology does not override the technical theological definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if every moment of your existence is a new gift from God, sustained by His will, or do you take your continued existence for granted? What would change if you genuinely believed that your very being is renewed each instant?
?
The arguments of ahl al-zahir seem reasonable but are refuted. Are there beliefs you hold that seem reasonable but contradict the deeper truths of tawhid? How can you guard against such errors?
?
If accidents do not persist, then nothing in creation is self-sustaining. How does this impact your trust in God versus trust in worldly means? Will you begin each day with a conscious reliance on Him alone?

The Qalb (Reversal) Refutation
The author uses reversal (qalb) to turn the opponent's argument back on itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the qalb (reversal) argument turns the opponent's own reasoning against him using the same disjunction of causes for annihilation.

Core Idea
Qalb defeats the opponent by using his own disjunction against him.

Sanusi now presents a powerful refutation technique called qalb (reversal). Instead of introducing new arguments, he takes the opponent's own disjunction of possible causes for an accident's annihilation and shows that, when applied consistently, it undermines the opponent's own position. This passage demonstrates the elegance and economy of the qalb method.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without parsing it deeply, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the rhythm and the structure of the reversal.
Notice that the passage begins with 'وأما القلب' (And as for the reversal).
2
Identify the disjunction used by the opponent
The passage lists four possible causes for annihilation: by itself, removal of condition, occurrence of a contrary, or existence of an efficient cause. Identify each one.
These are the same options the opponent had originally proposed.
3
Understand how the reversal works
Sanusi says that each of these options is invalid 'in the very same way mentioned' – meaning the opponent's own criticism applies to his own argument. Reflect on why this is a clever refutation.
The reversal does not require new premises; it turns the opponent's tools against him.
4
Apply the logic diagram
Study the flow diagram that maps the opponent's disjunction and the reversal. This will help visualize the logical structure.
Follow the arrows to see how the reversal leads back to the opponent's dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القلب فلأن العرض لو لم يبق ففناؤه أي عدمه عقيب الوجود، إما بنفسه أو غيره من زوال شرط، أو طريان ضد، أو وجود مؤثر، والكل باطل بعين ما ذكر.
And as for the reversal (qalb): Because if the accident does not endure, then its annihilation, i.e., its non-existence after existence, is either by itself or by another [cause] from the removal of a condition, or the occurrence of a contrary, or the existence of an efficient cause. And all of that is invalid in the very same way mentioned.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation by reversal (qalb). The opponent had argued that the non-endurance of accidents leads to a particular disjunction of causes for their annihilation. The author turns this around: if the accident does not endure, then the same disjunction applies, and each option is invalid for the same reasons the opponent had used. Thus the opponent's own argument is reversed against him.

Key Terms
قلب (qalb)
عرض ('arad)
فناء (fana')
عدم ('adam)
زوال شرط (zawal shart)
طريان ضد (turyan didd)
وجود مؤثر (wujud mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The qalb is just denying one of the premises of the opponent's argument.
Correction: The qalb actually uses the opponent's own disjunction and shows that his own reasoning leads to a contradiction when applied consistently, thus reversing the argument without introducing new premises.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the qalb (reversal) argument in this passage?
A) To prove that accidents do not endure.
B) To show that the opponent's disjunction leads to an infinite regress.
C) To turn the opponent's own disjunctive reasoning back against him.
D) To introduce a new set of causes for annihilation.

Key Takeaway
The qalb is a powerful dialectical tool that defeats an opponent by turning his own argument against him, demonstrating the internal consistency of one's own position without new evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you turned someone's argument back on them? Were you motivated by a desire for truth or by the need to win? How can you ensure that your use of logical tools like qalb serves justice and understanding rather than mere victory?
?
The qalb requires deep understanding of the opponent's position. Think of a person whose views you strongly disagree with. Have you ever taken the time to truly understand their reasoning, or do you dismiss it? What would change if you did?
?
Are there any beliefs you hold that, if you followed their logical implications consistently, would undermine themselves? Can you courageously examine your own position with the same scrutiny you apply to others?

Refuting the Argument that Accidents Perish by Themselves
First refutation: accident perishing by itself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the persistence of accidents and how the author refutes it.

Core Idea
An accident's self-annihilation is conditional and does not preclude existence.

The author begins by setting up the possible ways an accident could cease to exist. The first possibility is that it perishes by itself. The opponent argues that this would make its existence impossible. The author responds with a nuanced distinction, using the example of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory framework
Read the first part where the author lists four ways an accident might cease. Understand that these are the options the opponent holds, and the author rejects them all.
2
Examine the first argument and refutation
The opponent claims that if an accident perishes by itself, it would be impossible for it to exist. The author refutes by distinguishing between absolute and conditional self-annihilation.
Notice the example of motion: it necessitates non-existence after existence, but only under certain conditions.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author's key point: self-annihilation does not imply existence is impossible, because the accident's essence may require non-existence only after a period of persistence, not absolutely.
This prevents the opponent's objection from leading to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرض لو لم يبق ففناؤه أي عدمه عقيب الوجود، إما بنفسه أو غيره من زوال شرط، أو طريان ضد، أو وجود مؤثر، والكل باطل. وأما الحل فيمنع بعض مقدمات بيان إبطال أجزاء المنفصلة، وذلك من وجوه.

الأول: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بنفسه لكان ممتنع الوجود، وإنما يلزم لو اقتضى ذاته العدم مطلقاً، وأما إذا اقتضاه في بعض الأحوال كحال ما بعد البقاء فلا، وذلك كالحركة تقتضي العدم عقيب الوجود، غاية الأمر أن ترجح بعض الأوقات للزوال يفتقر إلى شرط لئلا يلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة.

والثاني: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بزوال شرط لزم الدور أو التسلسل لجواز أن يكون وجود العرض مشروطاً بوجود أعراض تتجدد في محالها على سبيل التبادل بأن يصير لاحقاً بدلاً عن سابق في الشرطية إلى أن ينتهي تلاحقهما إلى عرض لا يوجد الفاعل له بدلاً، فحينئذ يزول العرض المشروط بهذا الشرط بزوال شرط.

والثالث: لا نسلم أنه لو زال بطريان الضد لزم الدور المحال أو الترجح بلا مرجح.

أما الأول: فلأنه إن أريد بتوقف طريان الضد على زوال الآخر واشتراطه أن تحققه يحتاج إلى تحقق الزوال، والزوال مقدم عليه ولو بالذات ليكون تقدم الطريان عليه بالعلية دورانا لازمو ممنوع، وإن أريد أنه لا يفارقه أن يتحقق بدونه فالاستحالة ممنوعة، وذلك كدخول كل جزء من أجزاء في حيز الآخر وخروج الآخر عنه فإنه لا يتحقق أحدهما بدون الآخر من غير استحالة. نعم يكون للطريان سبق علته، وهو لا ينافي المعية الزمانية على أنه يجوز أن تكون العلة طريان الضد على المجاور، ويكون طريانه على المحل وزوال الباقي عنه معاً بحسب الذات، لا تقدم أحدهما على الآخر أصلاً.

وأما الثاني: فلجواز أن يكون الطارئ أقوى بحسب السبب فيرفع الباقي، ولا يندفع به، وإن تساويا في التضاد.

الرابع: لا نسلم أن العدم لا يصلح أثراً للفاعل، كيف وهو حادث يفتقر إلى محدث، والفاعل مقدم يلزم أن يكون أثره العدم، ولو سلم فنختار أنه ليس بفاعل، بمعنى أنه لا يفعل العرض أي يترك فعله لا بمعنى أن يفعل عدمه.

قال: والحق أن بقاء العرض في الجملة كبقاء الجسم لا سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس وليس التعويل على مجرد المشاهدة إذ الأمثال المتواردة قد تشاهد أمراً مستمراً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب. والحق يريد أن امتناع بقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق وإن كان مذهباً للأشاعرة وعليه يبتنى كثير من مطالبهم إلا أن الحق أن العلم ببقاء بعض الأعراض من الألوان والأشكال سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس كالعلوم والإدراكات وكثير من الملكات بمنزلة العلم ببقاء بعض الأجسام من غير فرق.
If the accident does not persist, then its annihilation, i.e., its non-existence after existence, is either by itself or by something else: by removal of a condition, or by the advent of an opposite, or by an efficient cause. But all are false. As for the solution, it prevents some premises of the demonstration of the invalidity of the disjunctive parts, and that is from several aspects.

First: We do not grant that if its passing away were by itself, then its existence would be impossible. That would only follow if its essence necessitated non-existence absolutely, but if it necessitated it in some states, like the state after persistence, then not. Such is motion: it necessitates non-existence after existence, except that the preponderance of a certain time for passing away requires a condition so that the effect does not lag behind the complete cause.

Second: We do not grant that if its passing away were by the removal of a condition, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, because it is possible that the existence of the accident is conditioned by the existence of accidents that are renewed in their places on a basis of exchange, such that a later accident becomes a substitute for an earlier one in conditionality until their alternation ends at an accident for which the agent does not create a substitute. Then the accident conditioned by this condition ceases due to the removal of the condition.

Third: We do not grant that if it ceased by the advent of an opposite, then impossible circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow.

As for the first [aspect]: If by the dependence of the advent of the opposite on the passing away of the other and its being conditioned that its actualization requires the actualization of the passing away, and the passing away is prior to it even in essence, so that the advent's priority over it by causality is a circularity whose necessary consequence is impossible, it is not granted. And if it is meant that it is inseparable that it occurs without it, then the impossibility is not granted. Such is the entering of each part into the space of the other and the exiting of the other from it, for neither occurs without the other, without impossibility. Indeed, the advent has the priority of its cause, which does not contradict temporal simultaneity, on the basis that it is possible that the cause is the advent of the opposite upon the adjacent [thing], and its advent upon the subject and the passing away of the remaining [accident] from it are simultaneous in essence, with no priority of one over the other at all.

As for the second [aspect]: Because it is possible that the adventitious [opposite] is stronger in terms of the cause, so it removes the remaining [accident] and it is not repelled by it, even if they are equal in opposition.

Fourth: We do not grant that non-existence is not suitable as an effect of the agent. How so? For it is an event and requires an originator, and the agent is a cause; it would necessarily follow that its effect is non-existence. And if it is granted, then we choose that it is not an agent, meaning that it does not produce the accident, i.e., it leaves off producing it, not that it produces its non-existence.

He said: The truth is that persistence of the accident in general is like persistence of the body, especially the accidents that subsist in the soul. And reliance is not on mere observation, for successive examples may be observed as a continuous thing like water poured from a spout. The truth intends that the impossibility of persistence of accidents absolutely, although it is a school of the Ash'aris and many of their claims are built upon it, yet the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents—colors and shapes, especially accidents subsisting in the soul like sciences, perceptions, and many dispositions—is akin to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies with no difference.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of four arguments that accidents cannot persist (baqa') and an affirmation of the truth that some accidents do persist. The author first presents the possible ways an accident might cease to exist (by itself, by removal of a condition, by an opposite, or by an efficient cause), then denies all of them.

First Objection: If the accident perished by itself, its existence would be impossible. The author replies: Not necessarily; it is like motion, which necessitates non-existence after existence but requires a condition for the preponderance of the time of cessation.

Second Objection: If it perished by removal of a condition, circularity or infinite regress would follow. The author replies: No, because the condition could be a series of alternating accidents that ends at one for which the agent creates no substitute.

Third Objection: If it perished by an opposite, circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow. The author gives two answers: (1) The dependence of the opposite's advent on the prior passing away is not a vicious circle—it is like two things that enter each other's space simultaneously. (2) The adventitious opposite might be stronger and thus remove the other, without equality requiring a preponderant.

Fourth Objection: Non-existence cannot be the effect of an agent. The author replies: Non-existence is an event and needs an originator; if the agent causes it, that is fine. If not, the agent simply ceases to produce the accident.

Conclusion (from 'He said'): The truth is that persistence of accidents in general is like persistence of bodies, especially those subsisting in the soul (mental states). One can know the persistence of some accidents (colors, shapes, knowledge, perceptions) just as one knows the persistence of bodies. The Ash'ari position that all accidents are non-persistent is not absolute truth; some are known to persist.

Key Terms
بقاء العرض
فناء العرض
زوال بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an accident perishes by itself, it cannot exist at all.
Correction: The author shows that self-annihilation can be conditional and does not preclude existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the author use as an example to illustrate conditional self-annihilation?
A) Fire
B) Motion
C) Sound
D) Light

Key Takeaway
An accident can perish by itself without making its existence impossible, as long as the self-annihilation is conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions do you hold that you treat as absolutely necessary, when in reality they may only be conditionally true? How would your perspective shift if you examined the conditions under which those assumptions hold?
?
Consider a time you dismissed a possibility as impossible without considering conditional scenarios. How might the distinction between absolute and conditional necessity help you approach such situations with more intellectual humility?
?
The author uses the example of motion to illustrate that something can necessitate non-existence after existence yet still exist. In your own spiritual journey, are there states or practices that you avoid because you fear they will 'annihilate' something good, when in reality they might only lead to transformation under certain conditions? What would it cost you to reexamine that fear?

Refuting the Argument of Removal of a Condition
Second refutation: accident perishing by removal of condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the persistence of accidents (removal of a condition) and how the author refutes it by allowing alternating conditions.

Core Idea
Removal of a condition can cause cessation without circularity if conditions alternate.

The opponent argues that if an accident ceases due to the removal of a condition, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress. The author responds by proposing a scenario where conditions are renewed in an alternating series, ending with an accident for which no substitute is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
The opponent claims that cessation by removal of a condition leads to circularity or infinite regress. Read the argument carefully.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author posits that the existence of the accident may be conditioned by a series of alternating accidents. Each successive accident replaces the previous one as a condition, until the series ends with a condition that has no replacement.
This avoids circularity because the chain is linear and finite.
3
Understand the implication
When the final condition lacks a substitute, the original accident ceases because its condition is removed. This is a valid scenario that defeats the opponent's objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرض لو لم يبق ففناؤه أي عدمه عقيب الوجود، إما بنفسه أو غيره من زوال شرط، أو طريان ضد، أو وجود مؤثر، والكل باطل. وأما الحل فيمنع بعض مقدمات بيان إبطال أجزاء المنفصلة، وذلك من وجوه.

الأول: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بنفسه لكان ممتنع الوجود، وإنما يلزم لو اقتضى ذاته العدم مطلقاً، وأما إذا اقتضاه في بعض الأحوال كحال ما بعد البقاء فلا، وذلك كالحركة تقتضي العدم عقيب الوجود، غاية الأمر أن ترجح بعض الأوقات للزوال يفتقر إلى شرط لئلا يلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة.

والثاني: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بزوال شرط لزم الدور أو التسلسل لجواز أن يكون وجود العرض مشروطاً بوجود أعراض تتجدد في محالها على سبيل التبادل بأن يصير لاحقاً بدلاً عن سابق في الشرطية إلى أن ينتهي تلاحقهما إلى عرض لا يوجد الفاعل له بدلاً، فحينئذ يزول العرض المشروط بهذا الشرط بزوال شرط.

والثالث: لا نسلم أنه لو زال بطريان الضد لزم الدور المحال أو الترجح بلا مرجح.

أما الأول: فلأنه إن أريد بتوقف طريان الضد على زوال الآخر واشتراطه أن تحققه يحتاج إلى تحقق الزوال، والزوال مقدم عليه ولو بالذات ليكون تقدم الطريان عليه بالعلية دورانا لازمو ممنوع، وإن أريد أنه لا يفارقه أن يتحقق بدونه فالاستحالة ممنوعة، وذلك كدخول كل جزء من أجزاء في حيز الآخر وخروج الآخر عنه فإنه لا يتحقق أحدهما بدون الآخر من غير استحالة. نعم يكون للطريان سبق علته، وهو لا ينافي المعية الزمانية على أنه يجوز أن تكون العلة طريان الضد على المجاور، ويكون طريانه على المحل وزوال الباقي عنه معاً بحسب الذات، لا تقدم أحدهما على الآخر أصلاً.

وأما الثاني: فلجواز أن يكون الطارئ أقوى بحسب السبب فيرفع الباقي، ولا يندفع به، وإن تساويا في التضاد.

الرابع: لا نسلم أن العدم لا يصلح أثراً للفاعل، كيف وهو حادث يفتقر إلى محدث، والفاعل مقدم يلزم أن يكون أثره العدم، ولو سلم فنختار أنه ليس بفاعل، بمعنى أنه لا يفعل العرض أي يترك فعله لا بمعنى أن يفعل عدمه.

قال: والحق أن بقاء العرض في الجملة كبقاء الجسم لا سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس وليس التعويل على مجرد المشاهدة إذ الأمثال المتواردة قد تشاهد أمراً مستمراً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب. والحق يريد أن امتناع بقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق وإن كان مذهباً للأشاعرة وعليه يبتنى كثير من مطالبهم إلا أن الحق أن العلم ببقاء بعض الأعراض من الألوان والأشكال سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس كالعلوم والإدراكات وكثير من الملكات بمنزلة العلم ببقاء بعض الأجسام من غير فرق.
If the accident does not persist, then its annihilation, i.e., its non-existence after existence, is either by itself or by something else: by removal of a condition, or by the advent of an opposite, or by an efficient cause. But all are false. As for the solution, it prevents some premises of the demonstration of the invalidity of the disjunctive parts, and that is from several aspects.

First: We do not grant that if its passing away were by itself, then its existence would be impossible. That would only follow if its essence necessitated non-existence absolutely, but if it necessitated it in some states, like the state after persistence, then not. Such is motion: it necessitates non-existence after existence, except that the preponderance of a certain time for passing away requires a condition so that the effect does not lag behind the complete cause.

Second: We do not grant that if its passing away were by the removal of a condition, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, because it is possible that the existence of the accident is conditioned by the existence of accidents that are renewed in their places on a basis of exchange, such that a later accident becomes a substitute for an earlier one in conditionality until their alternation ends at an accident for which the agent does not create a substitute. Then the accident conditioned by this condition ceases due to the removal of the condition.

Third: We do not grant that if it ceased by the advent of an opposite, then impossible circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow.

As for the first [aspect]: If by the dependence of the advent of the opposite on the passing away of the other and its being conditioned that its actualization requires the actualization of the passing away, and the passing away is prior to it even in essence, so that the advent's priority over it by causality is a circularity whose necessary consequence is impossible, it is not granted. And if it is meant that it is inseparable that it occurs without it, then the impossibility is not granted. Such is the entering of each part into the space of the other and the exiting of the other from it, for neither occurs without the other, without impossibility. Indeed, the advent has the priority of its cause, which does not contradict temporal simultaneity, on the basis that it is possible that the cause is the advent of the opposite upon the adjacent [thing], and its advent upon the subject and the passing away of the remaining [accident] from it are simultaneous in essence, with no priority of one over the other at all.

As for the second [aspect]: Because it is possible that the adventitious [opposite] is stronger in terms of the cause, so it removes the remaining [accident] and it is not repelled by it, even if they are equal in opposition.

Fourth: We do not grant that non-existence is not suitable as an effect of the agent. How so? For it is an event and requires an originator, and the agent is a cause; it would necessarily follow that its effect is non-existence. And if it is granted, then we choose that it is not an agent, meaning that it does not produce the accident, i.e., it leaves off producing it, not that it produces its non-existence.

He said: The truth is that persistence of the accident in general is like persistence of the body, especially the accidents that subsist in the soul. And reliance is not on mere observation, for successive examples may be observed as a continuous thing like water poured from a spout. The truth intends that the impossibility of persistence of accidents absolutely, although it is a school of the Ash'aris and many of their claims are built upon it, yet the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents—colors and shapes, especially accidents subsisting in the soul like sciences, perceptions, and many dispositions—is akin to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies with no difference.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of four arguments that accidents cannot persist (baqa') and an affirmation of the truth that some accidents do persist. Refuting the Argument of the Advent of an Opposite (1): Circularity and Preponderance
Third refutation: accident perishing by advent of opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument (advent of an opposite) and the author's two-fold refutation regarding circularity and preponderance.

Core Idea
The advent of an opposite does not necessitate circularity or preponderance without a preponderant.

The opponent argues that if an accident ceases due to the advent of an opposite, it leads to either a vicious circle or preponderance without a preponderant. The author provides two detailed responses, drawing on examples like spatial interpenetration and relative strength.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument and first sub-refutation
The first part addresses the claim of circularity. The author argues that the dependence of the opposite's advent on the prior cessation can be simultaneous in essence, not involving a vicious circle.
The example of two objects entering each other's space is given.
2
Read the second sub-refutation
The second part addresses the claim of preponderance without a preponderant. The author suggests that the adventitious opposite may be stronger and thus overcome the existing accident, which is not preponderance without cause.
3
Synthesize the responses
Combine both responses to see how the author defends the possibility of cessation by an opposite without logical flaws.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if two opposites are equal, neither can overcome the other without a preponderant, so cessation cannot occur.
Correction: The author states that the adventitious opposite may be stronger in cause, not necessarily in the accident itself, so it can overcome.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what allows the adventitious opposite to remove the remaining accident without a preponderant?
A) Equality of opposites
B) The adventitious opposite is stronger in cause
C) Temporal priority
D) Circular reasoning

Key Takeaway
The advent of an opposite can cause an accident to cease without circularity or requiring an external preponderant, because the advent and cessation can be simultaneous and the opposite may be stronger in cause.
